import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class FireModule(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_channels, squeeze_channels, expand1x1_channels, expand3x3_channels):
        """
        :param in_channels: Number of input channels
        :param squeeze_channels: Number of output channels for the squeeze layer
        :param expand1x1_channels: Number of output channels for the 1x1 expand layer
        :param expand3x3_channels: Number of output channels for the 3x3 expand layer
        """
        super(FireModule, self).__init__()
        
        self.squeeze = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, squeeze_channels, kernel_size=1)
        self.squeeze_activation = nn.ReLU(inplace=True)
        
        self.expand1x1 = nn.Conv2d(squeeze_channels, expand1x1_channels, kernel_size=1)
        self.expand1x1_activation = nn.ReLU(inplace=True)
        
        self.expand3x3 = nn.Conv2d(squeeze_channels, expand3x3_channels, kernel_size=3, padding=1)
        self.expand3x3_activation = nn.ReLU(inplace=True)
    
    def forward(self, x):
        """
        :param x: Input tensor, shape (batch_size, in_channels, height, width)
        :return: Output tensor, shape (batch_size, expand1x1_channels + expand3x3_channels, height, width)
        """
        x = self.squeeze_activation(self.squeeze(x))
        return torch.cat([
            self.expand1x1_activation(self.expand1x1(x)),
            self.expand3x3_activation(self.expand3x3(x))
        ], 1)

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, num_classes=1000):
        """
        :param num_classes: Number of output classes
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        self.features = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Conv2d(3, 96, kernel_size=7, stride=2),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=3, stride=2, ceil_mode=True),
            FireModule(96, 16, 64, 64),
            FireModule(128, 16, 64, 64),
            FireModule(128, 32, 128, 128),
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=3, stride=2, ceil_mode=True),
            FireModule(256, 32, 128, 128),
            FireModule(256, 48, 192, 192),
            FireModule(384, 48, 192, 192),
            FireModule(384, 64, 256, 256),
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=3, stride=2, ceil_mode=True),
            FireModule(512, 64, 256, 256),
        )
        
        self.classifier = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Dropout(p=0.0),
            nn.Conv2d(512, num_classes, kernel_size=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1, 1))
        )
    
    def forward(self, x):
        """
        :param x: Input tensor, shape (batch_size, 3, height, width)
        :return: Output tensor, shape (batch_size, num_classes)
        """
        x = self.features(x)
        x = self.classifier(x)
        return torch.flatten(x, 1)

# Test code
batch_size = 64
input_channels = 3
height = 512
width = 512
num_classes = 1000

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, input_channels, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [num_classes]

But wait - for the full network, we need the output of each Fire module to be stored for the next layer. Actually, maybe the biggest win is fusing multiple operations within a single "launch" or minimizing global memory traffic between layers. Let me think about this more systematically. Let me design a general `conv_relu` kernel for 1x1 and 3x3 convs: For 1x1 conv: ```cuda template<int C_in, int C_out> __global__ void conv1x1_relu( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, // [C_out, C_in] const float* __restrict__ bias, // [C_out] float* __restrict__ output, int B, int H, int W ) { int b = blockIdx.z; int h = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (h >= H || w >= W) return; int tid = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; // Load weight into shared memory? For small C_out * C_in, yes. __shared__ float s_weight[C_out][C_in]; // Cooperatively load weights... but C_out*C_in might be larger than block size. // Alternatively, each thread can load all weights it needs from global memory. // With L1 cache, repeated reads of weight are fast. int bhw_offset = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * C_in; float acc[C_out]; // register array #pragma unroll for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) acc[co] = bias[co]; #pragma unroll for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { float in_val = input[bhw_offset + ci]; #pragma unroll for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) { acc[co] += in_val * weight[co * C_in + ci]; } } int out_offset = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * C_out; #pragma unroll for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) { output[out_offset + co] = acc[co] > 0 ? acc[co] : 0; } } ``` This kernel has each thread compute one pixel for all C_out output channels. If C_out is large, the register usage per thread becomes high. For C_out=256, that's 256 floats = 1KB registers. With 1024 threads, that's 1MB registers, exceeding the per-SM limit of 64K*4 = 256KB registers. So we need to tile across output channels too. Each block could compute a subset of output channels. Let me redesign: - Each thread computes one output pixel for `C_out_per_thread` output channels - We use `blockDim.x * blockDim.y` threads per block, each handling one pixel - But then we can't handle all pixels if there are too many blocks... actually we can. Better design: - A thread block has `TILE_HW` threads (e.g., 256 or 512) that each handle one output pixel - The thread block computes all C_out channels for these pixels - Each thread iterates over C_out, accumulating one channel at a time, or a small group ```cuda template<int C_in, int C_out> __global__ void conv1x1_relu_v2( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int B, int H, int W ) { // Block: (BX, BY, B) where BX*BY = block size // Each thread handles one (h, w) and all output channels are computed cooperatively int b = blockIdx.z; int h = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (h >= H || w >= W) return; // Load input channels for this pixel float in_vals[C_in]; int in_base = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * C_in; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { in_vals[ci] = input[in_base + ci]; } // Compute each output channel int out_base = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * C_out; for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) { float acc = bias[co]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { acc += in_vals[ci] * weight[co * C_in + ci]; } output[out_base + co] = acc > 0 ? acc : 0; } } ``` For C_in=512, C_out=1002 (the final classifier conv), this would be slow because each thread does a lot of work. But for the Fire module channels, it might be okay. Wait, the final classifier is 512 -> 1000 classes, 1x1 conv. That would also be part of our custom kernel if we want to optimize everything. So where can we win? 1. **Operator fusion across multiple layers**: If we can fuse operations that require intermediate data to be written to and read from global memory, we save memory bandwidth. 2. **Avoiding Python overhead**: Not much to win here for a large model. 3. **Improving memory layout or using more efficient algorithms for specific cases**. Let me think about fusion more carefully. What operations can we fuse in SqueezeNet? Within a FireModule: ```python x = relu(conv1x1_squeeze(x_in)) out = cat([relu(conv1x1_expand(x)), relu(conv3x3_expand(x))]) ``` If we write one kernel for the entire FireModule: - For each output pixel: 1. Load input pixel channels (C_in values) 2. Compute squeeze output channels (C_squeeze values), apply relu 3. Compute expand1x1 output channels (C_e1 values), apply relu 4. Compute expand3x3 output channels (C_e2 values), apply relu (need 3x3 neighborhood of squeeze output) 5. Write concatenated output (C_e1 + C_e2 values) The issue is step 4: expand3x3 needs a 3x3 neighborhood of the squeeze output. If we're computing one output pixel at a time, the squeeze outputs for the 3x3 neighborhood are needed. For pixels in the interior, this is fine. But we'd be recomputing the squeeze values for the neighborhood instead of storing them. Actually, we can use a tile-based approach: - A thread block computes a tile of output pixels (e.g., 16x16) - First, all threads cooperatively compute the squeeze outputs for a slightly larger tile (18x18 to account for padding) - Store squeeze outputs in shared memory - Then compute expand1x1 and expand3x3 from shared memory - This saves global memory bandwidth for the squeeze output! This is the key idea. The PyTorch reference does: 1. conv_squeeze -> global memory write 2. relu_squeeze -> in-place, just touching global memory 3. conv_expand1x1 -> read squeeze output from global memory, compute, write to global memory 4. relu_expand1x1 -> in-place, global memory 5. conv_expand3x3 -> read squeeze output from global memory AGAIN, compute, write to global memory 6. relu_expand3x3 -> in-place, global memory 7. cat -> allocate new tensor, copy both outputs, write to global memory With our fused kernel: 1. Load input pixels from global memory 2. Compute squeeze outputs, store in shared memory 3. Compute expand1x1 outputs from shared memory, apply relu 4. Compute expand3x3 outputs from shared memory, apply relu 5. Write concatenated output to global memory This saves massive memory bandwidth for the intermediate squeeze output (which is read twice in PyTorch) and avoids the cat allocation/copy. For a FireModule with input 128 channels, squeeze 32, expand 128+128: - Squeeze output size per pixel: 32 floats - Memory saved: reading 32 floats twice (for expand1x1 and expand3x3) = 64 float reads per pixel - Plus writes for squeeze output = 32 float writes per pixel - Total saved: ~96 float memory ops per pixel Given H100 has 3.35 TB/s bandwidth, saving memory bandwidth could translate to significant speedup if the original was memory-bound. However, conv operations are typically compute-bound. But the intermediate storage/reading does add overhead. Actually, even if the convs themselves are compute-bound, the reduction in global memory traffic for intermediates and the elimination of separate kernel launches / Python overhead might add up across 11 Fire modules. Let me design this kernel more carefully. **Fused Fire Module Kernel:** ```cuda template<int C_in, int C_sq, int C_e1, int C_e2> __global__ void fire_module_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ sq_weight, const float* __restrict__ sq_bias, const float* __restrict__ e1_weight, const float* __restrict__ e1_bias, const float* __restrict__ e2_weight, const float* __restrict__ e2_bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int H, int W ) { const int TILE_H = 16; const int TILE_W = 16; // Pad for 3x3 conv const int SH = TILE_H + 2; const int SW = TILE_W + 2; // Shared memory: squeeze output tile + halo __shared__ float sm_sq[SH][SW][C_sq]; int b = blockIdx.z; int tile_h = blockIdx.y * TILE_H; int tile_w = blockIdx.x * TILE_W; int local_h = threadIdx.y; int local_w = threadIdx.x; // Compute global position int h = tile_h + local_h; int w = tile_w + local_w; // Load input and compute squeeze for all positions in the tile including halo // This is tricky because we need all threads to participate in computing the halo // Actually, let's think about this differently. Each thread block covers a tile. // We need squeeze values for [tile_h-1, tile_h+TILE_H] x [tile_w-1, tile_w+TILE_W] // For a 16x16 tile, we need 18x18 squeeze values, but only 16x16 output values. // We can have threads load multiple positions. For 256 threads (16x16), we need to compute 18*18=324 squeeze values. // So each thread computes ~1-2 squeeze values using the input. // Step 1: Compute squeeze outputs for all positions in the padded tile for (int idx = local_h * blockDim.x + local_w; idx < SH * SW; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int sh = idx / SW; int sw = idx % SW; int gh = tile_h - 1 + sh; int gw = tile_w - 1 + sw; if (gh >= 0 && gh < H && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { // Compute squeeze for this position int in_offset = ((b * H + gh) * W + gw) * C_in; for (int co = 0; co < C_sq; co++) { float acc = sq_bias[co]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { acc += input[in_offset + ci] * sq_weight[co * C_in + ci]; } sm_sq[sh][sw][co] = acc > 0 ? acc : 0; // ReLU } } } __syncthreads(); // Step 2: Compute expand1x1 and expand3x3 for valid output positions if (h < H && w < W) { int sh = local_h + 1; int sw = local_w + 1; int out_offset = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * (C_e1 + C_e2); // expand1x1 for (int co = 0; co < C_e1; co++) { float acc = e1_bias[co]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { acc += sm_sq[sh][sw][ci] * e1_weight[co * C_sq + ci]; } output[out_offset + co] = acc > 0 ? acc : 0; } // expand3x3 for (int co = 0; co < C_e2; co++) { float acc = e2_bias[co]; for (int dy = -1; dy <= 1; dy++) { for (int dx = -1; dx <= 1; dx++) { int w_offset = (dy * 3 + dx + 4) * C_sq; // e2_weight is [C_e2, C_sq, 3, 3] flattened // actually need to be careful with weight layout... for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { acc += sm_sq[sh + dy][sw + dx][ci] * e2_weight[co * C_sq * 9 + ci * 9 + (dy+1)*3 + (dx+1)]; } } } output[out_offset + C_e1 + co] = acc > 0 ? acc : 0; } } } ``` This is the general idea. Each Fire module can be a fused kernel that loads input, computes squeeze+relu into shared memory, then computes expand1x1+relu and expand3x3+relu, and writes concatenated output. Challenges: 1. Shared memory size: For C_sq=64, SH=18, SW=18, we need 18*18*64*4 bytes ≈ 82KB. That's within the 227KB shared memory limit per block on H100. 2. For larger C_sq (up to 64), it's fine. C_sq only goes up to 64 in this model. 3. Register pressure: Each thread needs to load C_in values, accumulate, etc. We need to be careful. Wait, actually in the kernel above, each thread computes squeeze for some positions (not its own output position, but all positions in the padded tile). Then all threads read from shared memory to compute their own output. Let me refine this. The issue is that threads might not have enough registers to hold everything. We should let the compiler handle register allocation by making the loops simple. Also, I need to be careful about the weight storage. 1x1 conv weights for squeeze: [C_sq, C_in]. For expand1x1: [C_e1, C_sq]. For expand3x3: [C_e2, C_sq, 3, 3]. If C_in is large (e.g., 512), loading all C_in values into registers might be too much. A thread computing one squeeze position needs to read all C_in input channels. With C_in=512, that's 512 float reads per position. A thread might compute 2 positions (since 324 positions / 256 threads ≈ 1.3). 512 reads per position is okay if coalesced. Actually, to better utilize memory bandwidth, we should parallelize across the channel dimension too. Let me rethink the kernel design: For the squeeze computation: - We have a spatial tile of 18x18 = 324 positions, each needing C_sq output channels - We can parallelize by having each thread handle one output channel for multiple positions, or one position for multiple output channels - For small C_sq (16, 32, 48, 64), we can have threads cooperatively compute all output channels for each position Similarly for the expands, we parallelize across output channels. Actually, for simplicity and to avoid register pressure, let me design the kernel with these parameters: - Block size: 16x16 threads = 256 threads - Tile size: 16x16 spatial pixels - Each thread is responsible for computing all output channels for its spatial pixel But for the squeeze step, we need the halo. So some threads compute halo squeeze values while others wait, or we parallelize the squeeze computation differently. Alternative approach: - Block size: 8x8 = 64 threads - Tile size: 8x8 spatial pixels - Padded tile: 10x10 = 100 positions - Each thread computes ~1-2 padded positions for squeeze - Then each thread computes its own output pixel's expand1x1 and expand3x3 With C_sq up to 64: - Shared memory for padded squeeze: 10*10*64*4 = 25.6KB - Each thread in the block handles one or two padded positions for squeeze computation - Then each thread computes one output pixel For the squeeze part, a thread computing a padded position would: - Load C_in input values (e.g., 96-512 floats) - Compute C_sq output values (e.g., 16-64 floats) - Write to shared memory If C_in=512, loading 512 floats per position is a lot. But with 64 threads each doing ~1-2 positions, and coalesced memory access, it might be okay. Actually, this might be too heavy per thread. Let me distribute the work differently. For squeeze: instead of having each thread compute all C_sq channels for a position, we can have the thread block compute all C_sq channels cooperatively. - For each padded position (sh, sw): - We need to compute C_sq output channels - We can have different threads handle different output channel ranges - But the total number of "work items" is SH*SW*C_sq = 100*64 = 6400 for the largest case - With 256 threads, each thread handles ~25 output channels - Each thread loops over all C_in input channels Wait, but then each thread needs to read C_in values. If 256 threads each read C_in values simultaneously, there will be some contention but also the reads will be cached. Actually, a more efficient approach for the squeeze step: - Load a block of input channels into shared memory - Compute partial dot products - Accumulate But given C_in might be 512 and our block size is small, this is complex. Let me think about a much simpler approach. What if I just write a fused kernel for: - conv1x1 + relu - conv3x3 + relu - maxpool And use them sequentially? The custom kernels save memory bandwidth by fusing conv with relu, and for 3x3 conv, maybe a custom kernel with shared memory loading of the input tile is competitive with cuDNN for small channel counts. Actually, for the custom conv1x1 + relu, I can write a kernel that is basically a manual GEMM but with ReLU fused. For small C_out, each thread can compute multiple output channels. Let me try a more practical approach. Instead of trying to be theoretically optimal, let me write the kernels, compile, and benchmark. I'll start with: 1. A simple conv1x1_relu kernel where each thread handles one output pixel and loops over output channels 2. A simple conv3x3_relu kernel with shared memory for the input tile 3. A maxpool kernel 4. Use PyTorch conv2d only for the initial 7x7 conv and maybe the final classifier Then I'll measure. If it's too slow, I'll iterate. Actually, wait. I realize I should think about whether PyTorch's eager mode is fusing conv+bias+relu. If it is, then my custom kernel might not help much unless I fuse more aggressively. In PyTorch eager mode, `nn.Conv2d` calls cuDNN's conv2d, which writes output to a tensor. Then `nn.ReLU(inplace=True)` does an in-place pass over that tensor. So: 1. cuDNN conv reads input and weights, writes output 2. ReLU reads and writes output This is 1 read of input + 1 write of output for conv, and 1 read + 1 write of output for relu. Total: read input once, read/write output twice. If cuDNN fuses conv+bias+relu (which it can do in some cases internally or in PyTorch's ATen), then it would be just 1 read input + 1 write output. But in eager mode, I don't think PyTorch automatically fuses conv+bias+relu into a single cuDNN call unless some optimization pass runs. Let me think... actually, in PyTorch, `nn.Conv2d` followed by `nn.ReLU()` might be handled by cuDNN's "fused conv + bias + relu" when using `torch.backends.cudnn.benchmark = True` or depending on the algorithm selection. Hmm, it's hard to know for sure. Let me just write the custom kernels and see. One more thing: the Fire module pattern. Even if each conv+relu is fused, the expand1x1 and expand3x3 both need to read the squeeze output. A custom kernel that fuses the entire Fire module avoids writing and then re-reading the squeeze output. So the fused Fire module kernel is definitely a good idea for saving memory bandwidth. Let me write a fused Fire module kernel design one more time, carefully: ```cuda template<int C_in, int C_sq, int C_e1, int C_e2, int block_h, int block_w> __global__ void fire_fused( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ w_sq, const float* __restrict__ b_sq, const float* __restrict__ w_e1, const float* __restrict__ b_e1, const float* __restrict__ w_e2, const float* __restrict__ b_e2, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int H, int W ) { const int SH = block_h + 2; const int SW = block_w + 2; // Shared memory for squeeze output tile with halo // Layout: [C_sq][SH][SW] to favor channel-first access within a thread // Actually [SH][SW][C_sq] might be better if multiple threads access different channels at same spatial position // Let me use [C_sq][SH][SW] so that a thread working on one output channel can access sequentially // Wait, no. For expand3x3, a thread needs ALL channels at the same spatial position. // Actually it's the opposite - a thread for a given spatial position needs all C_sq channels at that position. // So layout [SH][SW][C_sq] is better for a thread reading a spatial position. // But then threads in a warp accessing different spatial positions but same channel will have strided access. // Actually for expand3x3: // Each output pixel needs all C_sq channels at 3x3 neighborhood // If a thread computes one output pixel for one output channel, it needs C_sq inputs // If a thread computes one output pixel for all output channels, it needs C_sq inputs * C_e2 outputs // Let's have each thread compute ALL output channels for ONE spatial position. // Then for expand3x3, it needs all C_sq channels for the 3x3 neighborhood. // If C_sq=64, that's 576 values. Too many for registers. Must use shared memory. // So shared memory layout [SH][SW][C_sq] means a thread at (sh, sw) accesses // sm[sh][sw][0..C_sq-1], which is contiguous in memory if C_sq is the fastest varying dimension. // This should give good access patterns if multiple threads access same (sh, sw) but different channels. __shared__ float sm_sq[SH * SW * C_sq]; auto sm = [&](int sh, int sw, int c) -> float& { return sm_sq[(sh * SW + sw) * C_sq + c]; }; int b = blockIdx.z; int tile_h = blockIdx.y * block_h; int tile_w = blockIdx.x * block_w; int tid = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int ntid = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; // Step 1: Compute squeeze+relu for all positions in padded tile // Distribute SH*SW*C_sq work items across threads int total_sq = SH * SW * C_sq; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_sq; idx += ntid) { int c = idx % C_sq; int sw = (idx / C_sq) % SW; int sh = (idx / C_sq) / SW; int gh = tile_h - 1 + sh; int gw = tile_w - 1 + sw; if (gh >= 0 && gh < H && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { float acc = b_sq[c]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { acc += input[((b * H + gh) * W + gw) * C_in + ci] * w_sq[c * C_in + ci]; } sm(sh, sw, c) = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; } else { sm(sh, sw, c) = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Step 2: Compute output for this thread's position int h = tile_h + threadIdx.y; int w = tile_w + threadIdx.x; if (h < H && w < W) { int sh = threadIdx.y + 1; int sw = threadIdx.x + 1; int out_base = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * (C_e1 + C_e2); // Expand 1x1 for (int co = 0; co < C_e1; co++) { float acc = b_e1[co]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { acc += sm(sh, sw, ci) * w_e1[co * C_sq + ci]; } output[out_base + co] = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; } // Expand 3x3 for (int co = 0; co < C_e2; co++) { float acc = b_e2[co]; for (int dy = -1; dy <= 1; dy++) { for (int dx = -1; dx <= 1; dx++) { int w_idx = co * C_sq * 9 + (dy + 1) * 3 + (dx + 1); for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { acc += sm(sh + dy, sw + dx, ci) * w_e2[w_idx * C_sq + ci]; // if w_e2 is [C_e2, C_sq, 3, 3] in PyTorch, it's stored as: // w_e2[co, ci, dy+1, dx+1] // offset = ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + (dy+1)) * 3 + (dx+1) // = co*C_sq*9 + ci*9 + (dy+1)*3 + (dx+1) } } } output[out_base + C_e1 + co] = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; } } } ``` Wait, the weight layout for conv2d in PyTorch is (out_channels, in_channels, kH, kW). So w_e2[co, ci, dy+1, dx+1] is at offset ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + (dy+1)) * 3 + (dx+1). Let me double check the weight access in the squeeze computation. w_sq is for conv2d with C_out=C_sq, C_in=C_in, kH=1, kW=1. Layout: (C_sq, C_in, 1, 1). So w_sq[c, ci, 0, 0] is at offset c * C_in + ci. Good. For w_e1: (C_e1, C_sq, 1, 1). Offset: co * C_sq + ci. Good. For w_e2: (C_e2, C_sq, 3, 3). Offset: ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + dy) * 3 + dx for dy in [0,2], dx in [0,2]. In the 3x3 expansion loop, let dy range from -1 to 1 and dx from -1 to 1. The kernel spatial index in weight is (dy+1) and (dx+1). So the weight offset should be: ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + (dy+1)) * 3 + (dx+1) = co * C_sq * 9 + ci * 9 + (dy+1) * 3 + (dx+1) In my kernel, I should write this carefully. Now, will this kernel perform well? Let's analyze: 1. **Squeeze computation**: Distributed across threads. Each thread computes some (sh, sw, c) triples. For each triple, it loads all C_in input values. If C_in=512 and there are 64 threads, each thread handles ~total_sq/64 = 18*18*64/64 = 324 work items. Each work item loads 512 input values. But these loads are NOT coalesced across threads because threads with consecutive tid work on different (sh, sw, c), so they access different spatial positions and channels. Actually, for `idx = tid`, consecutive tids have consecutive `c` values for the same (sh, sw). They access the same spatial position but different weight channels. The input access is the same spatial position, different channels if we consider `input[... * C_in + ci]` where `ci` varies. Wait, in the squeeze computation, for a fixed (sh, sw, c), we load `input[spatial_offset * C_in + ci]` for ci = 0..C_in-1 and weight `w_sq[c * C_in + ci]`. Threads with consecutive `c` values access different weights but the same input. This might still work because input reads will be cached since many threads read the same input. Actually, the issue is that a thread computing one output channel (c) loads all C_in input channels for a spatial position. 256 threads each loading C_in values from the same spatial position means heavy cache contention, but it's cached so subsequent reads are fast. Hmm, but threads with different `(sh, sw)` access different spatial positions, which are far apart in memory. With C_in=512, each spatial position is 512*4=2KB apart. Consecutive threads might access positions 2KB apart, which is okay for L1 cache. Actually, I think the bigger issue is that each thread does a lot of work. 324 items * 512 reads each = ~165K reads per thread. That's a lot. Let me think about optimizing the squeeze step. If C_in is 512, and we have 256 threads, we could load input channels in a more cooperative way. Alternative: process one spatial position at a time. For each (sh, sw) in the padded tile: - Load all C_in input values for that position into shared memory - Then all threads compute their assigned output channels using the shared input - This way, input is loaded once per spatial position Let me redesign: ```cuda template<int C_in, int C_sq, int C_e1, int C_e2, int block_h, int block_w> __global__ void fire_fused_v2(...) { // Shared memory for input at current spatial position __shared__ float sm_input[C_in]; __shared__ float sm_sq[(block_h + 2) * (block_w + 2) * C_sq]; int b = blockIdx.z; int tile_h = blockIdx.y * block_h; int tile_w = blockIdx.x * block_w; // For each position in padded tile for (int sh = 0; sh < SH; sh++) { for (int sw = 0; sw < SW; sw++) { int gh = tile_h - 1 + sh; int gw = tile_w - 1 + sw; // Load input channels into shared memory if (gh >= 0 && gh < H && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { for (int ci = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; ci < C_in; ci += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { sm_input[ci] = input[((b * H + gh) * W + gw) * C_in + ci]; } } else { for (int ci = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; ci < C_in; ci += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { sm_input[ci] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute squeeze output channels for this position int c_per_thread = (C_sq + ntid - 1) / ntid; int c_start = tid * c_per_thread; int c_end = min(c_start + c_per_thread, C_sq); for (int c = c_start; c < c_end; c++) { float acc = b_sq[c]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { acc += sm_input[ci] * w_sq[c * C_in + ci]; } sm_sq[(sh * SW + sw) * C_sq + c] = acc > 0 ? acc : 0; } __syncthreads(); } } // ... then compute expands } ``` This version loads the input once per spatial position and all threads cooperative load it. Then each thread computes some output channels. For C_sq=64 and ntid=256, each thread computes at most 1 output channel. Memory for sm_input: up to 512 floats = 2KB. Memory for sm_sq: up to 18*18*64 = 20736 floats = 83KB. Total shared memory: ~85KB. That's fine for H100 (227KB limit). I like this design better. It's more cooperative. For the expand computation, we need each thread to access all C_sq channels at its spatial position. If we have each thread compute all output channels for one spatial position: - expand1x1: each output channel is a dot product of C_sq squeeze values with C_sq weights - expand3x3: each output channel is a dot product of 9*C_sq neighborhood values with weights For a thread at (h, w), it needs to iterate over output channels and compute the dot product. If C_sq=64, C_e1=256, C_e2=256: - expand1x1: 256 channels * 64 mul-adds each = 16K FLOPs - expand3x3: 256 channels * 576 mul-adds each = 147K FLOPs - Total per pixel: ~163K FLOPs With 256 threads per block and 16x16 tile (256 pixels), each thread does 163K FLOPs. That's reasonable if the thread has enough registers. Wait, in our current block design, block_h * block_w might be 256 threads. But actually if block_h=16, block_w=16, that's 256 threads, and each thread handles exactly one output pixel (since there are 256 pixels in a 16x16 tile). So each thread computes all C_e1 and C_e2 output channels for its one pixel. Register usage: - For expand1x1: accumulator array of C_e1 floats = up to 256 floats = 1KB registers - For expand3x3: accumulator array of C_e2 floats = up to 256 floats = 1KB registers - Plus loop variables - Total maybe 2-3KB per thread For 256 threads per block: 256 * 2.5KB = 640KB per block. H100 has 64K registers per SM = 256KB. 640KB > 256KB! So we can't have each thread compute all output channels if C_e1 and C_e2 are 256. We'll run out of registers. We need to tile across output channels too. We can process output channels in chunks. Each thread computes a subset of output channels, or we can have the block iterate over output channel tiles. Actually, a simpler approach: just have smaller tiles. If block is 8x8 = 64 threads, each thread handles one pixel, but now there are only 64 threads. Register usage per block would be 64 * 2.5KB = 160KB, which fits in 256KB. But with fewer threads, we have less parallelism and occupancy might be lower. Alternatively, we can have each thread compute a subset of output channels. For example, if C_e1=C_e2=256, each thread could compute 32 expand1x1 channels and 32 expand3x3 channels. Then we need 8 "sub-iterations" to cover all 256+256 channels. Actually, let me think about this differently. The compiler manages registers. If we write loops and let the compiler optimize, it might keep accumulators in registers automatically. But 256-512 floats is a lot. Actually, for blocks of 256 threads, H100 allows 255 registers per thread. 255 * 4 bytes = 1KB per thread. We absolutely cannot store 512 floats in registers. So we MUST process output channels in chunks. Let me redesign: ```cuda // Each block has BLOCK_HW threads // Each thread handles one spatial pixel // We process C_out channels in groups of C_OUT_TILE template<int C_in, int C_sq, int C_e1, int C_e2, int BH, int BW, int CO_TILE> __global__ void fire_fused(...) { __shared__ float sm_input[C_in]; __shared__ float sm_sq[(BH+2)*(BW+2)*C_sq]; // ... squeeze computation same as before ... // Expand computation, processed in tiles int h = tile_h + threadIdx.y; int w = tile_w + threadIdx.x; if (h >= H || w >= W) return; int sh = threadIdx.y + 1; int sw = threadIdx.x + 1; int out_base = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * (C_e1 + C_e2); // Expand 1x1 in tiles for (int co_tile = 0; co_tile < C_e1; co_tile += CO_TILE) { int co_end = min(co_tile + CO_TILE, C_e1); float acc[CO_TILE]; // compiler might put this in registers or local memory for (int co = co_tile; co < co_end; co++) { acc[co - co_tile] = b_e1[co]; } for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { float sq_val = sm_sq[(sh * (BW+2) + sw) * C_sq + ci]; for (int co = co_tile; co < co_end; co++) { acc[co - co_tile] += sq_val * w_e1[co * C_sq + ci]; } } for (int co = co_tile; co < co_end; co++) { float val = acc[co - co_tile]; output[out_base + co] = val > 0 ? val : 0; } } // Expand 3x3 in tiles, similar // ... } ``` But CO_TILE can't be too large. If CO_TILE=16, acc[16] = 64 bytes. That's fine. For expand3x3, we need a 3x3 neighborhood. We can load the required neighborhood values for each channel. But we can process it similarly in tiles. Wait, there's another optimization. For expand3x3, a thread needs 9*C_sq values from shared memory. We can process it one output channel at a time to minimize register usage. Actually, for expand3x3, it's more efficient to process one output channel fully before moving to the next. This way we only need one accumulator register. ```cuda for (int co = 0; co < C_e2; co++) { float acc = b_e2[co]; for (int dy = -1; dy <= 1; dy++) { for (int dx = -1; dx <= 1; dx++) { int w_base = ((co * C_sq) * 3 + (dy+1)) * 3 + (dx+1); for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { acc += sm_sq[((sh+dy)*(BW+2) + (sw+dx)) * C_sq + ci] * w_e2[w_base * C_sq + ci]; // wait, w_e2 layout: w_e2[co, ci, dy+1, dx+1] // offset = ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + (dy+1)) * 3 + (dx+1) } } } output[out_base + C_e1 + co] = acc > 0 ? acc : 0; } ``` Wait, I need to be careful about the weight offset. In PyTorch, Conv2d weight is [out_channels, in_channels, kH, kW]. So for expand3x3 with out=C_e2, in=C_sq, kH=3, kW=3: - w_e2[co, ci, kh, kw] where kh in [0,2], kw in [0,2] - Linear offset: ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw In the kernel: ```cuda for (int dy = -1; dy <= 1; dy++) { int kh = dy + 1; for (int dx = -1; dx <= 1; dx++) { int kw = dx + 1; int w_base = ((co * C_sq) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { acc += sm_sq[... + ci] * w_e2[w_base + ci * 9]; // wait no: ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw = co*C_sq*9 + ci*9 + kh*3 + kw // So for fixed co, kh, kw: offset = co*C_sq*9 + kh*3 + kw + ci*9 } } } ``` So for fixed co and spatial offset (kh, kw), the weights for ci=0,1,2... are spaced 9 apart. That's strided access. Actually, if we rearrange the loops: ```cuda for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int w_offset = ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw; acc += sm_sq[...][ci] * w_e2[w_offset]; } } } ``` This has better locality for weights but worse for sm_sq (since we load the same spatial position 9 times for each ci). Alternatively, we can restructure shared memory to be [C_sq][SH][SW] instead of [SH][SW][C_sq]. Then for a given ci, the 3x3 neighborhood is contiguous or nearly contiguous. Actually [C_sq][SH][SW] means sm_sq[ci][sh][sw] is at offset (ci * SH + sh) * SW + sw. For fixed ci and sh, sw varies contiguously. For fixed ci and varying sh, there's a stride of SW. If a thread processes one output pixel and needs a 3x3 neighborhood for one ci at a time: - sm_sq[ci][sh-1..sh+1][sw-1..sw+1] This has good spatial locality if SW is small and the 3x3 fits in cache lines. But loading different ci values means jumping by SH*SW in memory. If we process ci sequentially, that's fine. Actually, for expand3x3 at one output pixel, the most natural order is: ```cuda for (int co = 0; co < C_e2; co++) { float acc = b_e2[co]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int w_offset = ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw; acc += sm_sq[(ci * SH + sh + kh - 1) * SW + sw + kw - 1] * w_e2[w_offset]; } } } output[...] = fmaxf(acc, 0.0f); } ``` This requires 256 threads per block to each load 576 shared memory values (for C_sq=64, 3x3=9). Since shared memory is fast and the accesses should be cached within a warp (all threads in a warp read the same 3x3 values for the same ci), this should be efficient due to broadcast in shared memory. Wait, all threads in a warp read different spatial positions in the 3x3 neighborhood around their own pixel. If the block processes a 16x16 tile, thread (0,0) reads positions (-1,-1) to (1,1), thread (0,1) reads (-1,0) to (1,2), etc. Within a warp, the read addresses are somewhat scattered across shared memory. But with SW=18 and 16x16 threads, adjacent threads read adjacent sw values, so there should be good locality. Let me also reconsider the shared memory layout for sm_sq. If we use [C_sq][SH][SW], then for fixed ci and sh, sw varies linearly. For 3x3 reads where sw varies by +/-1, adjacent threads in a warp will access adjacent memory locations. This is great for memory coalescing. Actually, for [C_sq][SH][SW], the offset is `ci * SH * SW + sh * SW + sw`. For blockDim.x=16 (sw=threadIdx.x), sh=threadIdx.y, ci varies: - `base + sw` where `base = ci*SH*SW + sh*SW` - For a fixed warp (e.g., sh=0, sw=0..15), adjacent threads access adjacent memory. Good. - For the 3x3 neighborhood: threads access base + sw + dx offsets. Still adjacent. So [C_sq][SH][SW] layout works well. OK let me now think about which tiling parameters to use: - For small feature maps (e.g., 32x32 after multiple poolings), we want enough blocks to saturate the GPU - For large feature maps (127x127), 16x16 tiles give us 8x8 = 64 blocks per image, and batch=64 gives 4096 blocks total. H100 has many SMs, so this should be plenty. Let me compute: H100 has ~132 SMs. With 4096 blocks, each SM gets ~31 blocks. That's good. For the Fire module, the largest padded shared memory is for C_sq=64, block=16x16: - sm_input: C_in up to 512 * 4 = 2KB - sm_sq: 64 * 18 * 18 * 4 = 82.9KB - Total: ~85KB For C_sq=64 with block=16x16, each block has 256 threads. If each thread uses ~64 registers (256 bytes), total registers = 64KB per block. H100 allows up to 255 registers/thread = 1KB/thread max, but practical limit is lower. Actually, register pressure is the main concern. If we process outputs in small tiles (e.g., CO_TILE=8 for expand1x1), register usage per thread is manageable. For expand1x1 with CO_TILE: ```cuda for (int co = 0; co < C_e1; co += CO_TILE) { float acc[CO_TILE]; for (int i = 0; i < CO_TILE && co+i < C_e1; i++) acc[i] = b_e1[co+i]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { float v = sm_sq[ci * SH * SW + sh * SW + sw]; for (int i = 0; i < CO_TILE && co+i < C_e1; i++) { acc[i] += v * w_e1[(co+i) * C_sq + ci]; } } for (int i = 0; i < CO_TILE && co+i < C_e1; i++) { output[out_base + co + i] = acc[i] > 0 ? acc[i] : 0; } } ``` If CO_TILE=8, acc[8] = 32 bytes. That's very reasonable. For expand3x3, I'll process one output channel at a time to minimize registers: ```cuda for (int co = 0; co < C_e2; co++) { float acc = b_e2[co]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { int sm_base = ci * SH * SW; for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int w_offset = ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw; acc += sm_sq[sm_base + (sh + kh) * SW + sw + kw] * w_e2[w_offset]; } } } output[out_base + C_e1 + co] = acc > 0 ? acc : 0; } ``` This only needs 1 accumulator register for expand3x3. Great. OK, I think this kernel design is solid. Now let me think about which Fire modules to apply it to, and how to handle the rest of the network. The architecture is: 1. OK, let me now decide on an implementation plan: **Plan A**: Use PyTorch conv2d for the large initial conv, and write custom fused kernels for: 1. `conv1x1_relu`: 1x1 conv + bias + relu (used for squeeze in Fire modules and classifier conv) 2. `conv3x3_relu`: 3x3 conv + padding=1 + bias + relu (used for expand3x3 in Fire modules) 3. `fire_module`: Fully fused squeeze + expand1x1 + expand3x3 + cat (for Fire4, Fire5, Fire6, Fire7, Fire8) Wait, but if I write separate `conv1x1_relu` and `conv3x3_relu`, I'm not fusing across the Fire module operations. Let me think about memory access patterns for the squeeze step with the cooperative loading design: ```cuda for (int sh = 0; sh < SH; sh++) { for (int sw = 0; sw < SW; sw++) { int gh = tile_h - 1 + sh; int gw = tile_w - 1 + sw; // Coop load C_in channels for (int ci = tid; ci < C_in; ci += ntid) { if (gh >= 0 && gh < H && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { sm_input[ci] = input[((b * H + gh) * W + gw) * C_in + ci]; } else { sm_input[ci] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute squeeze channels for (int c = tid; c < C_sq; c += ntid) { float acc = b_sq[c]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { acc += sm_input[ci] * w_sq[c * C_in + ci]; } sm_sq[(sh * SW + sw) * C_sq + c] = acc > 0 ? acc : 0; } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Wait, there's a problem here. The `sm_input` array needs to be sized to `C_in`, but `C_in` is a template parameter. That's fine. However, in the inner loop over `sh` and `sw`, all threads in the block participate in loading `sm_input`, then computing squeeze outputs. The synchronization ensures correctness. But the loop over `sh` and `sw` processes each spatial position in the padded tile sequentially. For SH*SW=324 positions and C_in=512: - Loading 512 floats per position = 324 * 512 * 4 = ~663KB of input reads per block - But these are from global memory, not all cached - Actually, adjacent positions in the tile share many input pixels (since 1x1 conv reads only one position, adjacent padded positions read adjacent global memory) - But we load them sequentially, so by the time we get to the next position, the previous data is likely evicted from L1 cache! Oh no, that's right. We're loading C_in=512 floats from a new spatial position each iteration. With 324 iterations, we're doing 324 * 512 ≈ 166K global memory loads per block. That's terrible because we lose cache locality between adjacent spatial positions. For a 1x1 conv, adjacent output positions need adjacent input positions. There's no reuse between positions. So loading sequentially is fine, except we're doing it 324 times. The total input reads are the same as before. But actually, we can optimize by having threads load multiple spatial positions at once. Or we can restructure: instead of iterating over spatial positions, we can iterate over output channels. Wait, the issue is that for the Fire module, output positions don't share input values (it's 1x1 conv for squeeze). So each output position needs its own input values loaded. Total input reads = SH * SW * C_in. That's the minimum required. But the way we're doing it, for each of the 324 positions, 256 threads each load ~2 channels (since 512/256 = 2). So it takes 2 iterations to load all channels. The loads are coalesced because consecutive threads load consecutive channels. So per position, we do 2 rounds of loads. Total global memory transactions: 324 * 2 = 648 transactions per block (warp-sized). That's actually not bad. The total memory read from global memory for input is SH * SW * C_in = 324 * 512 = 165,888 floats. With batch=64 and 127x127 image, how many blocks are there? For 16x16 tiles, ~8x8 = 64 blocks per image, 64 images = 4096 blocks. Total input reads across all blocks: 4096 * 165,888 ≈ 679M floats ≈ 2.7GB. This matches the input feature map size (64 * 512 * 127 * 127 * 4 = 2.08GB) times the padded ratio (324/256 = 1.27), so ~2.6GB. Reasonable. OK, this seems correct. The memory bandwidth is used efficiently. But actually, I realize we can do better for the halo positions! For interior positions in the tile, we're loading them one by one. But if we process the entire padded tile cooperatively, all threads load their assigned channels for ALL positions, then compute. Let me think about this. Alternative squeeze computation: - Assign each thread in the block to compute some output channels for ALL padded positions - But then each thread needs to read all C_in input values for each position, which is C_in * SH * SW reads per thread - This is worse than cooperative loading The cooperative loading approach where we load sm_input once per position and reuse it across threads computing different output channels is actually optimal for 1x1 conv in terms of global memory reads. Actually, I just realized: instead of loading one position at a time, we can load multiple positions' input vectors into shared memory simultaneously if shared memory is large enough. No padding needed for squeeze!) - Wait, I made an error. For the Fire module, squeeze is 1x1 conv with NO padding. The output size is the same as input size. Expand1x1 is 1x1, no padding. Expand3x3 has padding=1, so output size is the same. - So for input HxW, all operations produce HxW output. The padded tile is only needed for expand3x3, because we need a 3x3 neighborhood. So SH = block_h + 2, SW = block_w + 2. For block=8x8: SH=SW=10. For C_in=512: sm_input size = 512*4=2KB, but we'd need to load 100 positions. OK wait, I think the position-by-position approach is still correct. Actually, let me reconsider. For the squeeze step, since it's a 1x1 conv, we don't actually need to load each position sequentially with `__syncthreads()`. We can have all threads work independently: For each (sh, sw) position and each c output channel: - Thread idx = (sh, sw, c) mapped to thread ID - Compute squeeze for that (position, channel) - This requires loading all C_in input values for that position With 256 threads in the block and 100 positions * 64 channels = 6400 work items, each thread does 6400/256 = 25 work items. Each work item loads 512 input values. Total loads per thread = 25 * 512 = 12,800 floats. In parallel across 256 threads, total loads = 3.3M floats per block. But these loads are from global memory for 100 different positions. Since positions are adjacent, some caching might help, but not perfectly. The cooperative loading approach (loading sm_input once per position for all channels) is better because each input float is loaded exactly once per position, regardless of how many output channels are computed. So position-by-position with cooperative loading is the way to go. Now, for expand3x3, we need a 3x3 neighborhood per output pixel. Can we tile this efficiently too? Now let me write the PyTorch forward pass structure: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, num_classes=1000): super().__init__() self.num_classes = num_classes # First conv self.conv1 = nn.Conv2d(3, 96, kernel_size=7, stride=2) # Fire module weights - we need to create these as parameters # We can create them manually or copy from the reference self.fire_params = nn.ParameterList() # Actually, to match the reference exactly and ensure grad computation, # we should keep the same nn.Conv2d modules and extract their weights/biases # But then we're carrying around the nn.Conv2d modules just for their weights # That should be fine. # Let me create all the conv layers self.sq_convs = nn.ModuleList() self.e1_convs = nn.ModuleList() self.e2_convs = nn.ModuleList() configs = [ (96, 16, 64, 64), (128, 16, 64, 64), (128, 32, 128, 128), (256, 32, 128, 128), (256, 48, 192, 192), (384, 48, 192, 192), (384, 64, 256, 256), (512, 64, 256, 256), ] for cin, csq, ce1, ce2 in configs: self.sq_convs.append(nn.Conv2d(cin, csq, 1)) self.e1_convs.append(nn.Conv2d(csq, ce1, 1)) self.e2_convs.append(nn.Conv2d(csq, ce2, 3, padding=1)) self.classifier_conv = nn.Conv2d(512, num_classes, kernel_size=1) def forward(self, x): x = F.relu(self.conv1(x)) x = F.max_pool2d(x, kernel_size=3, stride=2, ceil_mode=True) for i in range(8): x = fire_cuda_forward(x, self.sq_convs[i].weight, self.sq_convs[i].bias, self.e1_convs[i].weight, self.e1_convs[i].bias, self.e2_convs[i].weight, self.e2_convs[i].bias, i) # pass index to select the right kernel x = F.max_pool2d(x, kernel_size=3, stride=2, ceil_mode=True) x = classifier_cuda_forward(x, self.classifier_conv.weight, self.classifier_conv.bias) return x ``` Wait, I need to handle the MaxPool between Fire modules correctly. For the first conv, F.conv2d output should be contiguous. So I probably don't need `.contiguous()` calls, but adding them is safer. However, `.contiguous()` on an already-contiguous tensor is basically a no-op. Now, one more thing: when I use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`, the module needs to compile. This can take time, but it only happens once at import. Let me draft the full code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template<int C_in, int C_sq, int C_e1, int C_e2, int BH, int BW> __global__ void fire_fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ w_sq, const float* __restrict__ b_sq, const float* __restrict__ w_e1, const float* __restrict__ b_e1, const float* __restrict__ w_e2, const float* __restrict__ b_e2, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int H, int W ) { const int SH = BH + 2; const int SW = BW + 2; const int ntid = BH * BW; const int tid = threadIdx.y * BW + threadIdx.x; const int b = blockIdx.z; const int tile_h = blockIdx.y * BH; const int tile_w = blockIdx.x * BW; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* sm_input = smem; float* sm_sq = smem + C_in; // Step 1: Compute squeeze+relu for padded tile for (int sh = 0; sh < SH; sh++) { for (int sw = 0; sw < SW; sw++) { int gh = tile_h - 1 + sh; int gw = tile_w - 1 + sw; // Cooperative load input channels if (gh >= 0 && gh < H && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { for (int ci = tid; ci < C_in; ci += ntid) { sm_input[ci] = input[((b * H + gh) * W + gw) * C_in + ci]; } } else { for (int ci = tid; ci < C_in; ci += ntid) { sm_input[ci] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute squeeze output channels for this position for (int c = tid; c < C_sq; c += ntid) { float acc = b_sq[c]; #pragma unroll for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { acc += sm_input[ci] * w_sq[c * C_in + ci]; } sm_sq[(sh * SW + sw) * C_sq + c] = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); } } // Step 2: Compute output for this thread's pixel int h = tile_h + threadIdx.y; int w = tile_w + threadIdx.x; if (h >= H || w >= W) return; int sh = threadIdx.y + 1; int sw = threadIdx.x + 1; int out_base = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * (C_e1 + C_e2); int sq_offset = (sh * SW + sw) * C_sq; // Expand 1x1 + ReLU for (int co = 0; co < C_e1; co++) { float acc = b_e1[co]; #pragma unroll for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { acc += sm_sq[sq_offset + ci] * w_e1[co * C_sq + ci]; } output[out_base + co] = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; } // Expand 3x3 + ReLU for (int co = 0; co < C_e2; co++) { float acc = b_e2[co]; #pragma unroll for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { int sm_base = ci * SH * SW; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int w_offset = ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw; acc += sm_sq[sm_base + (sh + kh) * SW + sw + kw] * w_e2[w_offset]; } } } output[out_base + C_e1 + co] = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; } } // Classifier: 1x1 conv + relu + global avg pool template<int C_in, int C_out, int BH, int BW> __global__ void classifier_fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int H, int W ) { __shared__ float sm_input[C_in]; __shared__ float sm_acc[C_out]; int b = blockIdx.z; int tile_h = blockIdx.y * BH; int tile_w = blockIdx.x * BW; int tid = threadIdx.y * BW + threadIdx.x; int ntid = BH * BW; // Initialize accumulators for (int c = tid; c < C_out; c += ntid) { sm_acc[c] = 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); // For each spatial position in this tile for (int local_h = 0; local_h < BH; local_h++) { for (int local_w = 0; local_w < BW; local_w++) { int h = tile_h + local_h; int w = tile_w + local_w; if (h >= H || w >= W) continue; // Load input int base = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * C_in; for (int ci = tid; ci < C_in; ci += ntid) { sm_input[ci] = input[base + ci]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute conv1x1+relu and accumulate for (int co = tid; co < C_out; co += ntid) { float acc = bias[co]; #pragma unroll for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { acc += sm_input[ci] * weight[co * C_in + ci]; } acc = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; sm_acc[co] += acc; } __syncthreads(); } } // Write partial sums to output (will need reduction) // Actually, better approach: each block handles one (b, tile_h, tile_w) // and we atomically add to output[b, c] for (int co = tid; co < C_out; co += ntid) { atomicAdd(&output[b * C_out + co], sm_acc[co]); } } // Wrapper functions at::Tensor fire_forward_generic(...) { // ... } ''' ``` Wait, for the classifier, using atomicAdd means all blocks for the same batch item will accumulate to the same output. We need to: 1. Zero-initialize output before the kernel 2. Launch blocks over all spatial tiles 3. Each block atomically adds its partial sum 4. After kernel, divide by H*W This should work. But atomicAdd on FP32 can have precision issues if many blocks contribute. With tiles of 8x8 on a 32x32 image: 4x4 = 16 blocks per image. 16 atomic adds per channel. Not many, so precision should be fine. Actually wait, I realize the classifier is on x with shape [B, 512, H, W] where H=W is the output of the last MaxPool. Let me trace through: After Fire7 (512->64->256+256), output is [B, 512, H, W]. Fire7 input was after the 3rd MaxPool. Input to model: 512x512 After conv7x7/s2: 253x253 After maxpool/2: 127x127 After maxpool/2: 63x63 After maxpool/2: 31x31 Wait, let me compute the exact sizes with ceil_mode=True: - Input: 512 - conv7x7/s2: floor((512 - 7)/2) + 1 = floor(505/2) + 1 = 252 + 1 = 253 - maxpool3x3/s2 ceil_mode: ceil(253/2) = 127 - Fire0-2: 127x127 - maxpool: ceil(127/2) = 64 - Fire3-6: 64x64 - maxpool: ceil(64/2) = 32 - Fire7: 32x32 For the classifier conv: 1x1 on 32x32 global avg pool: mean over 32x32 = 1024 So H=W=32 at the classifier. For atomicAdd with 16 blocks per image: 16 adds per channel. That should be fine. But actually, there's an even simpler approach. Let me write a simple conv1x1_relu kernel: ```cuda template<int C_in, int C_out> __global__ void conv1x1_relu_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int H, int W ) { int b = blockIdx.z; int h = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (h >= H || w >= W) return; int in_base = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * C_in; int out_base = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * C_out; for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) { float acc = bias[co]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { acc += input[in_base + ci] * weight[co * C_in + ci]; } output[out_base + co] = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; } } ``` Each thread computes all C_out channels for one pixel. For the squeeze step in the Fire module kernel, I iterate over padded positions (SH*SW positions). For each position, I load C_in inputs and compute C_sq outputs. For large C_in (512) and small C_sq (64), each thread computes at most 1 output channel per position (since ntid=64 and C_sq=64). That's efficient - each thread does at most 1 output channel per position. The issue is that within one position iteration, threads with consecutive `tid` compute consecutive output channels, accessing the same `sm_input` but different weights. This is fine - broadcast on shared memory. For the expand steps, each thread computes all output channels for one spatial pixel. For C_e1=256 and C_e2=256, each thread does 256+256=512 output channels. With C_sq=64: - Expand1x1: 256 * 64 = 16,384 FMAs - Expand3x3: 256 * 64 * 9 = 147,456 FMAs - Total: ~164K FMAs per thread For a block of 64 threads, total FMAs per block = 64 * 164K = 10.5M. At 67 TFLOPS, 10.5M / 67T = 0.00016 seconds = 0.16ms. But with partial SM utilization due to multiple blocks, maybe 0.5ms? This should still be reasonable. But register pressure might be high for expand1x1 because the compiler might keep all 256 accumulators in registers. 256 floats = 1KB registers. Per thread. For 64 threads: 64KB per block. H100 has 64K registers per SM, but each register is 4 bytes, so 256KB total. 64KB is fine. Wait, 64K registers per SM means 64K * 4 bytes = 256KB. And each block uses 64KB. So we can run up to 4 blocks per SM. With 64 threads per block, that's 256 threads per SM. H100 supports 2048 threads per SM, so we're well within limits. Actually, 64KB / 64 threads = 1KB per thread = 256 registers per thread. The maximum is 255 registers per thread, so we're right at the limit! The compiler might spill some values to local memory if it can't fit everything in 255 registers. To reduce register pressure, I can tile the output channel computation. For example, process 32 output channels at a time. ```cuda for (int co_base = 0; co_base < C_e1; co_base += 32) { float acc[32]; for (int i = 0; i < 32 && co_base + i < C_e1; i++) acc[i] = b_e1[co_base + i]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { float v = sm_sq[sq_offset + ci]; for (int i = 0; i < 32 && co_base + i < C_e1; i++) { acc[i] += v * w_e1[(co_base + i) * C_sq + ci]; } } for (int i = 0; i < 32 && co_base + i < C_e1; i++) { output[out_base + co_base + i] = acc[i] > 0.0f ? acc[i] : 0.0f; } } ``` With CO_TILE=32, acc[32] = 128 bytes. That's 32 registers, leaving plenty for other variables. For expand3x3, since I process one output channel at a time, register pressure is naturally low (just 1 accumulator). Let me refine the kernel to use CO_TILE=32 for expand1x1. But I need to be careful with how the compiler handles the array. If CO_TILE is a template parameter, the compiler can unroll the loops, but if it's a runtime variable, it can't. Since C_e1 is a template parameter, I can use it to determine the tile size or just use a fixed tile like 32. Actually, for different C_e1 values (64, 128, 192, 256), CO_TILE=32 works well. Here's my plan for the code structure: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template<int C_in, int C_sq, int C_e1, int C_e2, int BH, int BW, int CO_TILE> __global__ void fire_fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ w_sq, const float* __restrict__ b_sq, const float* __restrict__ w_e1, const float* __restrict__ b_e1, const float* __restrict__ w_e2, const float* __restrict__ b_e2, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int H, int W ) { const int SH = BH + 2; const int SW = BW + 2; const int ntid = BH * BW; const int tid = threadIdx.y * BW + threadIdx.x; const int b = blockIdx.z; const int tile_h = blockIdx.y * BH; const int tile_w = blockIdx.x * BW; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* sm_input = smem; float* sm_sq = smem + C_in; // Squeeze computation for padded tile #pragma unroll 1 for (int sh = 0; sh < SH; sh++) { #pragma unroll 1 for (int sw = 0; sw < SW; sw++) { int gh = tile_h - 1 + sh; int gw = tile_w - 1 + sw; if (gh >= 0 && gh < H && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { for (int ci = tid; ci < C_in; ci += ntid) { sm_input[ci] = input[((b * H + gh) * W + gw) * C_in + ci]; } } else { for (int ci = tid; ci < C_in; ci += ntid) { sm_input[ci] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); for (int c = tid; c < C_sq; c += ntid) { float acc = b_sq[c]; #pragma unroll for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { acc += sm_input[ci] * w_sq[c * C_in + ci]; } sm_sq[(sh * SW + sw) * C_sq + c] = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); } } int h = tile_h + threadIdx.y; int w = tile_w + threadIdx.x; if (h >= H || w >= W) return; int sh = threadIdx.y + 1; int sw = threadIdx.x + 1; int out_base = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * (C_e1 + C_e2); int sq_off = (sh * SW + sw) * C_sq; // Expand 1x1 + ReLU for (int co_base = 0; co_base < C_e1; co_base += CO_TILE) { float acc[CO_TILE]; int co_end = min(co_base + CO_TILE, C_e1); for (int co = co_base; co < co_end; co++) { acc[co - co_base] = b_e1[co]; } for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { float v = sm_sq[sq_off + ci]; for (int co = co_base; co < co_end; co++) { acc[co - co_base] += v * w_e1[co * C_sq + ci]; } } for (int co = co_base; co < co_end; co++) { output[out_base + co] = acc[co - co_base] > 0.0f ? acc[co - co_base] : 0.0f; } } // Expand 3x3 + ReLU for (int co = 0; co < C_e2; co++) { float acc = b_e2[co]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { int sm_base = ci * SH * SW; for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int w_offset = ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw; acc += sm_sq[sm_base + (sh + kh) * SW + sw + kw] * w_e2[w_offset]; } } } output[out_base + C_e1 + co] = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; } } // Wrapper functions for each Fire module #define DEFINE_FIRE_WRAPPER(name, CIN, CSQ, CE1, CE2) \ at::Tensor name( \ at::Tensor input, \ at::Tensor w_sq, at::Tensor b_sq, \ at::Tensor w_e1, at::Tensor b_e1, \ at::Tensor w_e2, at::Tensor b_e2) { \ int B = input.size(0); \ int H = input.size(2); \ int W = input.size(3); \ auto output = torch::empty({B, CE1 + CE2, H, W}, input.options()); \ const int BH = 8; \ const int BW = 8; \ const int CO_TILE = 32; \ dim3 block(BW, BH); \ dim3 grid((W + BW - 1) / BW, (H + BH - 1) / BH, B); \ int sm_size = (CIN + (BH+2)*(BW+2)*CSQ) * sizeof(float); \ fire_fused_kernel<CIN, CSQ, CE1, CE2, BH, BW, CO_TILE><<<grid, block, sm_size>>>( \ input.data_ptr<float>(), \ w_sq.data_ptr<float>(), b_sq.data_ptr<float>(), \ w_e1.data_ptr<float>(), b_e1.data_ptr<float>(), \ w_e2.data_ptr<float>(), b_e2.data_ptr<float>(), \ output.data_ptr<float>(), \ B, H, W); \ return output; \ } DEFINE_FIRE_WRAPPER(fire_forward_0, 96, 16, 64, 64) DEFINE_FIRE_WRAPPER(fire_forward_1, 128, 16, 64, 64) DEFINE_FIRE_WRAPPER(fire_forward_2, 128, 32, 128, 128) DEFINE_FIRE_WRAPPER(fire_forward_3, 256, 32, 128, 128) DEFINE_FIRE_WRAPPER(fire_forward_4, 256, 48, 192, 192) DEFINE_FIRE_WRAPPER(fire_forward_5, 384, 48, 192, 192) DEFINE_FIRE_WRAPPER(fire_forward_6, 384, 64, 256, 256) DEFINE_FIRE_WRAPPER(fire_forward_7, 512, 64, 256, 256) // Classifier conv1x1 + relu kernel template<int C_in, int C_out, int BH, int BW, int CO_TILE> __global__ void conv1x1_relu_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int H, int W ) { int b = blockIdx.z; int h = blockIdx.y * BH + threadIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.x * BW + threadIdx.x; if (h >= H || w >= W) return; int in_base = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * C_in; int out_base = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * C_out; for (int co_base = 0; co_base < C_out; co_base += CO_TILE) { float acc[CO_TILE]; int co_end = min(co_base + CO_TILE, C_out); for (int co = co_base; co < co_end; co++) { acc[co - co_base] = bias[co]; } for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { float v = input[in_base + ci]; for (int co = co_base; co < co_end; co++) { acc[co - co_base] += v * weight[co * C_in + ci]; } } for (int co = co_base; co < co_end; co++) { output[out_base + co] = acc[co - co_base] > 0.0f ? acc[co - co_base] : 0.0f; } } } at::Tensor conv1x1_relu_forward( at::Tensor input, at::Tensor weight, at::Tensor bias, int C_out) { int B = input.size(0); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int C_in = input.size(1); auto output = torch::empty({B, C_out, H, W}, input.options()); const int BH = 8; const int BW = 8; const int CO_TILE = 32; dim3 block(BW, BH); dim3 grid((W + BW - 1) / BW, (H + BH - 1) / BH, B); // We need to dispatch based on C_in and C_out // For the classifier: C_in=512, C_out=1000 (or num_classes) if (C_in == 512 && C_out == 1000) { conv1x1_relu_kernel<512, 1000, BH, BW, CO_TILE><<<grid, block>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), B, H, W); } else { // Generic fallback using PyTorch output = torch::relu(torch::conv2d(input, weight, bias)); } return output; } ''' cpp_source = r''' // The wrapper functions are defined in the CUDA source ''' cuda_ext = load_inline( name='squeezenet_cuda', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE, functions=[ 'fire_forward_0', 'fire_forward_1', 'fire_forward_2', 'fire_forward_3', 'fire_forward_4', 'fire_forward_5', 'fire_forward_6', 'fire_forward_7', 'conv1x1_relu_forward' ], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'] ) ``` Wait, there's an issue with the macro approach for template instantiation. The `DEFINE_FIRE_WRAPPER` macro creates a function, but the template parameters for the kernel are passed directly. This should work. But actually, `fire_fused_kernel` is a template, and the macro instantiates it with specific values. That's fine. However, there's an issue with the `CO_TILE` template parameter in `acc[CO_TILE]`. If CO_TILE=32 and C_e1=64, the compiler can handle it. But `acc[CO_TILE]` where CO_TILE is a template parameter should be fine for small values. Wait, one issue: when C_e1 < CO_TILE (e.g., C_e1=64 and CO_TILE=32, or C_e1=64 and CO_TILE=64). With CO_TILE=32, C_e1=64 loops twice, each time with co_end=32 then 64. That's fine. For `acc[CO_TILE]`, the array is declared on the stack. With CO_TILE=32, it's 32 floats. The compiler should put this in registers. Another issue: `#pragma unroll` on the loop `for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++)`. If C_in=512, unrolling this loop would create a huge function. The compiler probably won't fully unroll 512 iterations, but `#pragma unroll` with a large constant might cause the compiler to partially unroll or ignore it. I should use `#pragma unroll` for small loops (like C_sq=16,32,64) but maybe not for C_in=512. Actually, `#pragma unroll` without a number means fully unroll. For C_in=512, that's too much. I should change it to `#pragma unroll 8` or remove it for C_in. For the C_sq loops, since C_sq is 16, 32, 48, or 64, unrolling is fine. For C_in, I'll either: 1. Not use `#pragma unroll` 2. Use `#pragma unroll 4` or `#pragma unroll 8` Actually, `#pragma unroll` is just a hint. The compiler decides whether to unroll based on register pressure and code size. I'll leave it for small loops and omit or use `#pragma unroll 4` for large loops. Let me write the final code. I need to be careful about the weight layout for expand3x3. In PyTorch: ```python conv = nn.Conv2d(C_sq, C_e2, 3, padding=1) weight = conv.weight # shape [C_e2, C_sq, 3, 3] ``` In CUDA, `w_e2[((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw]` accesses weight[co, ci, kh, kw]. This is correct. Now, in the Python ModelNew class: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, num_classes=1000): super().__init__() self.num_classes = num_classes self.conv1 = nn.Conv2d(3, 96, kernel_size=7, stride=2) # Fire modules (store as separate conv layers for weights) self.fire_sq = nn.ModuleList() self.fire_e1 = nn.ModuleList() self.fire_e2 = nn.ModuleList() configs = [ (96, 16, 64, 64), (128, 16, 64, 64), (128, 32, 128, 128), (256, 32, 128, 128), (256, 48, 192, 192), (384, 48, 192, 192), (384, 64, 256, 256), (512, 64, 256, 256), ] for c_in, c_sq, c_e1, c_e2 in configs: self.fire_sq.append(nn.Conv2d(c_in, c_sq, 1)) self.fire_e1.append(nn.Conv2d(c_sq, c_e1, 1)) self.fire_e2.append(nn.Conv2d(c_sq, c_e2, 3, padding=1)) self.classifier_conv = nn.Conv2d(512, num_classes, kernel_size=1) def forward(self, x): x = F.relu(self.conv1(x)) x = F.max_pool2d(x, kernel_size=3, stride=2, ceil_mode=True) fire_kernels = [ cuda_ext.fire_forward_0, cuda_ext.fire_forward_1, cuda_ext.fire_forward_2, cuda_ext.fire_forward_3, cuda_ext.fire_forward_4, cuda_ext.fire_forward_5, cuda_ext.fire_forward_6, cuda_ext.fire_forward_7, ] for i, fire_fn in enumerate(fire_kernels): x = fire_fn( x.contiguous(), self.fire_sq[i].weight.contiguous(), self.fire_sq[i].bias.contiguous(), self.fire_e1[i].weight.contiguous(), self.fire_e1[i].bias.contiguous(), self.fire_e2[i].weight.contiguous(), self.fire_e2[i].bias.contiguous() ) # MaxPool after fire modules 2, 5 if i == 2 or i == 5: x = F.max_pool2d(x, kernel_size=3, stride=2, ceil_mode=True) x = cuda_ext.conv1x1_relu_forward( x.contiguous(), self.classifier_conv.weight.contiguous(), self.classifier_conv.bias.contiguous(), self.num_classes ) # Dropout with p=0.0 is no-op x = F.adaptive_avg_pool2d(x, (1, 1)) return torch.flatten(x, 1) ``` Wait, I need to check where the maxpools occur: - After conv1 - After Fire2 (index 2 in 0-based) - After Fire6 (index 6 in 0-based)? For the shared memory accesses in expand3x3: ```cpp for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { int sm_base = ci * SH * SW; for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int w_offset = ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw; acc += sm_sq[sm_base + (sh + kh) * SW + sw + kw] * w_e2[w_offset]; } } } ``` For C_sq=64, this is 64*9 = 576 iterations. No, each thread computes a different `co` (output channel). So different threads read different weights. For a fixed `ci`, threads with different `co` read weights at offsets `co * C_sq * 9 + ...`. If C_sq=64, the stride between consecutive `co` weights is 64 * 9 = 576 floats = 2.3KB. When threads in a warp read weights with stride 2.3KB, these are not contiguous. A warp of 32 threads would read 32 * 2.3KB = 73.6KB, which exceeds cache line sizes. This will result in 32 separate memory transactions for the weights. If C_e2=256, each warp handles 32 different output channels. The weight reads are highly strided and might not be well cached.
