I made a bug in my expand3x3 shared memory access! Let me fix it. The correct access for `sm_sq` at spatial `(sh, sw)` and channel `ci` is: ```cpp sm_sq[(sh * SW + sw) * C_sq + ci] ``` So the expand3x3 loop should be: ```cpp for (int co = 0; co < C_e2; co++) { float acc = b_e2[co]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int w_offset = ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw; acc += sm_sq[((sh + kh) * SW + sw + kw) * C_sq + ci] * w_e2[w_offset]; } } } output[out_base + C_e1 + co] = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; } ``` Similarly, for expand1x1: ```cpp for (int co = 0; co < C_e1; co++) { float acc = b_e1[co]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { acc += sm_sq[(sh * SW + sw) * C_sq + ci] * w_e1[co * C_sq + ci]; } output[out_base + co] = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; } ``` For spatial access within a warp: - Expand1x1: all threads read `sm_sq[(sh * SW + sw) * C_sq + ci]`. With `sh` varying by thread, and the layout being `(sh * SW + sw) * C_sq + ci`, threads in a warp read different `sh` values. Since `sh` differs by 1, the memory access has stride `SW * C_sq`. For `SW=10, C_sq=64`, stride = 640 floats = 2.56KB. Not great for shared memory coalescing. But shared memory is banked, so as long as the stride isn't a multiple of 32, consecutive threads access different banks and there's no conflict. Actually, shared memory in H100 has 32 banks (4 bytes each, so 128 bytes per bank set). With stride 640 floats = 2560 bytes, thread 0 accesses bank (0*2560 / 4) % 32 = 0, thread 1 accesses bank 640 % 32 = 0... wait, 640 % 32 = 0. Uh oh! 640 is divisible by 32. This means `threadIdx.x` consecutive threads all access the same bank for different `sh` values! Wait, let me compute more carefully. For expand1x1, threads in a warp all have different `(sh, sw)` values. The offset in `sm_sq` is `(sh * SW + sw) * C_sq + ci`. For threads with the same `sh` but different `sw` (e.g., tids 0-7 all have sh=0, sw=0-7): - offset difference between thread 0 and thread 1: `(0*SW + 1)*C_sq - (0*SW + 0)*C_sq = C_sq = 64 floats = 256 bytes`. - Bank: 256 / 4 = 64. 64 % 32 = 0. - Thread 0: bank 0. Thread 1: bank 0 (because 64 % 32 = 0). - Thread 2: bank 128 % 32 = 0. Wait, all threads in the same row access the same bank for different channels? Actually no, for a FIXED `ci`, the offset for thread with `sw` is `(sh * SW + sw) * C_sq + ci`. The difference between adjacent `sw` threads is `C_sq = 64` floats. `64 * 4 = 256 bytes`. `256 / 4 = 64`. Bank index = 64 % 32 = 0. Oh no - ALL threads in the same row access bank 0! But they're accessing different memory addresses within bank 0, which means bank conflicts! Actually, in Ampere/Hopper, shared memory has 32 banks of 4 bytes. A bank conflict occurs when multiple threads in a warp access different addresses within the same bank. With C_sq=64 floats = 256 bytes, thread 0 accesses offset 0 → bank 0, thread 1 accesses offset 64 → bank 0, thread 2 accesses offset 128 → bank 0, etc. All threads in a row access bank 0 but different addresses. This is a 32-way bank conflict per warp! This is terrible and will serialize the shared memory accesses. To avoid bank conflicts, we need `C_sq % 32 != 0` or the offset between adjacent threads to hit different banks. But C_sq is 16, 32, 48, 64, some of which are multiples of 32. Wait, for C_sq=16: offset difference = 16 floats = 64 bytes. Bank = 64 / 4 = 16. 16 % 32 = 16. So threads 0 and 1 access banks 0 and 16. Threads 2 and 3 access banks... wait, thread with sw=2: offset = 32 floats = 128 bytes. Bank = 128 / 4 = 32. 32 % 32 = 0. So threads 0, 2, 4, ... access bank 0, and threads 1, 3, 5, ... access bank 16. Still a 16-way bank conflict. For C_sq=32: offset difference = 32 floats = 128 bytes. Bank = 128 / 4 = 32. 32 % 32 = 0. All threads in the same row access bank 0. 32-way bank conflict. For C_sq=48: offset difference = 48 floats = 192 bytes. Bank = 192 / 4 = 48. 48 % 32 = 16. Threads 0, 2, 4, ... bank 0; threads 1, 3, 5, ... bank 16. 16-way conflict. For C_sq=64: offset difference = 64 floats = 256 bytes. Bank = 256 / 4 = 64. 64 % 32 = 0. 32-way conflict. Yikes! All cases have bank conflicts because C_sq * 4 is divisible by 128 (the bank width in bytes). Wait, bank width in Ampere/Hopper is 4 bytes for 32-bit accesses. So the bank index is `(offset_in_bytes / 4) % 32`. Offset difference between adjacent `sw` threads: `C_sq * 4` bytes. Bank difference: `(C_sq * 4 / 4) % 32 = C_sq % 32`. For C_sq=16: 16 % 32 = 16. So even and odd threads hit different banks, but still 16 threads hit the same bank. For C_sq=32: 32 % 32 = 0. All threads hit the same bank! For C_sq=48: 48 % 32 = 16. Same as 16. For C_sq=64: 64 % 32 = 0. All threads hit the same bank! This is terrible. I need to fix the shared memory layout. To avoid bank conflicts, I can pad the channel dimension. Instead of storing `C_sq` channels contiguously, I can pad to `C_sq_padded` where `C_sq_padded % 32 == 1` or some value that distributes accesses. Or better yet, I can change the shared memory layout to `[C_sq][SH][SW]`. Then for a fixed `ci` and `sh`, adjacent `sw` threads access adjacent memory locations: - offset = `ci * SH * SW + sh * SW + sw` - Adjacent thread difference = 1 float = 4 bytes - Bank difference = 1 % 32 = 1 This means consecutive threads access consecutive banks: thread 0 bank 0, thread 1 bank 1, thread 2 bank 2, etc. NO bank conflicts! Wait, but in my squeeze computation, I stored the channels as `(sh * SW + sw) * C_sq + c`. If I switch to `c * SH * SW + sh * SW + sw`, I need to change the squeeze storage and the expand reads. Let me redesign with `[C_sq][SH][SW]` layout: Squeeze storage: ```cpp sm_sq[c * SH * SW + sh * SW + sw] = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; ``` Expand1x1 reads: ```cpp for (int co = 0; co < C_e1; co++) { float acc = b_e1[co]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { acc += sm_sq[ci * SH * SW + sh * SW + sw] * w_e1[co * C_sq + ci]; } output[...] = fmaxf(acc, 0.0f); } ``` Expand3x3 reads: ```cpp for (int co = 0; co < C_e2; co++) { float acc = b_e2[co]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int w_offset = ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw; acc += sm_sq[ci * SH * SW + (sh + kh) * SW + sw + kw] * w_e2[w_offset]; } } } output[...] = fmaxf(acc, 0.0f); } ``` For expand1x1, threads in the same row (same `sh`, different `sw`) access: - `sm_sq[ci * SH * SW + sh * SW + sw]` - Shift by `sw`, so adjacent threads have offset difference of 1 float = 4 bytes, which means consecutive banks. Perfect, no bank conflicts! For expand3x3, threads access `sw + kw` where `kw` is fixed within the inner loop. Same pattern: adjacent threads differ by 1 in `sw`, so 1 float = 4 bytes, consecutive banks. No bank conflicts! This layout is much better. But the squeeze storage needs to change: In squeeze step: ```cpp for (int c = tid; c < C_sq; c += ntid) { float acc = b_sq[c]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { acc += sm_input[ci] * w_sq[c * C_in + ci]; } sm_sq[c * SH * SW + sh * SW + sw] = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; } ``` Here, threads with consecutive `c` (output channel) write to memory separated by `SH * SW` floats (e.g., 100 floats = 400 bytes). Bank = 400 / 4 = 100. 100 % 32 = 4. Threads with c=0,1,2,3 write to banks 0,4,8,12,16,20,24,28,... No conflicts! But wait, different threads write to the same spatial position `(sh, sw)` but different channels. Since each thread writes to a unique `c` value, there are no write conflicts as long as consecutive threads write to different banks (which they do, since bank offset = `SH*SW % 32`). For SH=SW=10: SH*SW=100. 100 % 32 = 4. So consecutive `c` values hit banks 0, 4, 8, 12, 16, 20, 24, 28, 0, 4, ... No conflicts because each thread writes a unique address. Actually, wait. Threads with `c=0` and `c=8` hit bank `(0 + 8*100) % 32 = 800 % 32 = 0`. They both hit bank 0! But they write to different addresses within bank 0. Is that a bank conflict? For writes, yes, multiple threads writing to the same bank (even different addresses) causes serialization. But `c=0` and `c=8` are 8 apart in thread index. If `ntid=64`, the threads are in the same warp? Actually, warp has 32 threads. So threads with c=0 and c=8 are in the same warp if 8 < 32. Threads in warp: c values = tid, tid+ntid, tid+2*ntid, ... Since ntid=64, in the first call, tid ranges from 0 to 63. The warp processes tids 0-31 simultaneously. These correspond to c values: 0, 1, 2, ..., 31. Bank for c=0: (0*100) % 32 = 0 Bank for c=1: 100 % 32 = 4 Bank for c=2: 200 % 32 = 8 Bank for c=3: 300 % 32 = 12 Bank for c=4: 400 % 32 = 16 Bank for c=5: 500 % 32 = 20 Bank for c=6: 600 % 32 = 24 Bank for c=7: 700 % 32 = 28 Bank for c=8: 800 % 32 = 0 Oh, c=0 and c=8 both hit bank 0. Within the same warp! Write conflict. With SH*SW=100 and 32 banks: 100 % 32 = 4. Every 8 consecutive c values, we cycle through all 32 banks. So in a warp of 32 threads (c=0..31), c=0 and c=8 both hit bank 0, c=1 and c=9 hit bank 4, etc. This is a 4-way bank conflict for writes. Hmm, 4-way conflict is not terrible. Shared memory can handle a few conflicts. And for the expand reads, the layout is perfect. Actually, to eliminate conflicts entirely, I could pad `SH*SW` to a value coprime with 32. For example, pad `SH*SW` to 101. Then bank for c: (c * 101) % 32 = (c * 5) % 32. This cycles through all banks! For c=0..31: 0, 5, 10, 15, 20, 25, 30, 3, 8, 13, 18, 23, 28, 1, 6, 11, 16, 21, 26, 31, 4, 9, 14, 19, 24, 29, 2, 7, 12, 17, 22, 27. All different! No conflicts! But padding to 101 when we only need 100 wastes a tiny bit of shared memory (0.4KB for C_sq=64). That's totally fine! Actually, I can use `SH * SW + 1` as the stride to ensure no bank conflicts. But `SH*SW` is a compile-time constant for each template instance. For SH=SW=10: 101. For SH=SW=12: 145? Wait, SH=10, SW=10: 100. I want to pad to 101. Alternatively, I can use `C_sq` as the fastest varying dimension but pad `C_sq` to a multiple of 32+1 or something. That's more complex. Actually, a simpler fix: in the original layout `(sh * SW + sw) * C_sq + c`, the expand reads had bank conflicts. In the new layout `c * SH * SW + sh * SW + sw`, the expand reads are perfect but the squeeze writes have mild conflicts. Given that expand reads happen many more times (C_e1 + C_e2 output channels per pixel, each doing C_sq reads), while squeeze writes happen once per block per padded position, I'd rather optimize expand reads. 4-way bank conflict on squeeze writes is acceptable. Let me use the `c * SH * SW + sh * SW + sw` layout. But wait, can I pad the spatial dimension to avoid write conflicts too? Use `c * (SH * SW + 1) + sh * SW + sw`. Then bank for c is `c * (SH*SW+1) % 32`. For SH*SW=100: `c * 101 % 32`. As computed above, this gives no conflicts! Shared memory increase: C_sq * 1 extra float = up to 64 floats = 256 bytes. Negligible! So I'll use: `c * (SH * SW + 1) + sh * SW + sw` for the squeeze storage. Actually, for simplicity, let me define a stride constant: ```cpp const int SQ_STRIDE = SH * SW + 1; // or some padding to avoid bank conflicts ``` But to keep the code readable, I'll just accept a small amount of bank conflict or use a simpler approach. Actually, you know what? On H100 (Hopper), shared memory has a lot of bandwidth, and bank conflicts for writes are less of an issue. And 4-way conflict is really not that bad. Let me just use `c * SH * SW + sh * SW + sw` without padding. The code is cleaner and the performance impact is minimal. If profiling shows that shared memory is a bottleneck, I can add padding later. One more thing: for the expand3x3 reads, when threads in the same warp access the 3x3 neighborhood, they access different spatial positions. With the layout `c * SH * SW + sh * SW + sw`: - For `kw=0`: threads read `sw + 0` to `sw + 0`, which differs by 1 between adjacent threads → no conflict - For `kw=1`: threads read `sw + 1` → also differs by 1 between adjacent threads → no conflict - Same for `kw=2` But wait, for `sh + kh`, if `kh > 0`, then `sh` differs between threads. If `sh` differs by 1, then `sh * SW` differs by `SW`. For SW=10, `sh * SW` differs by 10 floats = 40 bytes. Bank difference = 40 / 4 = 10. 10 % 32 = 10. So for adjacent threads that differ in `sh` (e.g., thread in row 0 and row 1), their `sh*SW` values differ by SW. For SW=10, that's bank 10 apart. No conflict. What about threads in the same row but different `sh` values? They don't exist in a warp since all threads in the warp have the same `sh` if the warp covers a row. Wait, warp 0 covers rows 0-3 with 8 columns each. Threads in the same warp have different `sh` values. Actually, in expand3x3, for a fixed `kh`, a thread with `sh=0` accesses `sh + kh = kh`, and a thread with `sh=1` accesses `1 + kh`. Their memory offsets differ by `SW` floats. Bank difference = `SW % 32`. For SW=10, that's 10. No conflict between any threads! Great! The `[C_sq][SH][SW]` layout has excellent access patterns for expand reads. OK, I'll use this layout. Let me update the kernel design. Also, for the expand1x1 reads, all threads in the block with the same `sh` and different `sw` access addresses differing by 1 float. Perfect! Now one more consideration: for the squeeze step, when we iterate over padded positions, the same thread accesses different spatial positions in `sm_sq` for different `(sh, sw)` iterations. Since the write to `sm_sq` is done once per position and the spatial positions are handled sequentially (not in parallel by the same thread), there's no write conflict within a single `__syncthreads()` epoch. Good. Now let me think about whether I should use templates for `BH` and `BW` too. Since they're always 8, I can hardcode them or keep them as templates. Hardcoding is simpler. Final kernel design: ```cpp template<int C_in, int C_sq, int C_e1, int C_e2> __global__ void fire_fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ w_sq, const float* __restrict__ b_sq, const float* __restrict__ w_e1, const float* __restrict__ b_e1, const float* __restrict__ w_e2, const float* __restrict__ b_e2, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int H, int W ) { const int BH = 8; const int BW = 8; const int SH = BH + 2; const int SW = BW + 2; const int ntid = BH * BW; const int tid = threadIdx.y * BW + threadIdx.x; const int b = blockIdx.z; const int tile_h = blockIdx.y * BH; const int tile_w = blockIdx.x * BW; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* sm_input = smem; float* sm_sq = smem + C_in; // Squeeze computation for padded tile for (int sh = 0; sh < SH; sh++) { for (int sw = 0; sw < SW; sw++) { int gh = tile_h - 1 + sh; int gw = tile_w - 1 + sw; if (gh >= 0 && gh < H && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { for (int ci = tid; ci < C_in; ci += ntid) { sm_input[ci] = input[((b * H + gh) * W + gw) * C_in + ci]; } } else { for (int ci = tid; ci < C_in; ci += ntid) { sm_input[ci] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); for (int c = tid; c < C_sq; c += ntid) { float acc = b_sq[c]; #pragma unroll 4 for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { acc += sm_input[ci] * w_sq[c * C_in + ci]; } sm_sq[c * SH * SW + sh * SW + sw] = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); } } int h = tile_h + threadIdx.y; int w = tile_w + threadIdx.x; if (h >= H || w >= W) return; int sh = threadIdx.y + 1; int sw = threadIdx.x + 1; int out_base = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * (C_e1 + C_e2); int sq_spatial = sh * SW + sw; // Expand 1x1 + ReLU for (int co_base = 0; co_base < C_e1; co_base += 32) { float acc[32]; int co_end = min(co_base + 32, C_e1); for (int co = co_base; co < co_end; co++) { acc[co - co_base] = b_e1[co]; } for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { float v = sm_sq[ci * SH * SW + sq_spatial]; #pragma unroll for (int co = co_base; co < co_end; co++) { acc[co - co_base] += v * w_e1[co * C_sq + ci]; } } for (int co = co_base; co < co_end; co++) { output[out_base + co] = acc[co - co_base] > 0.0f ? acc[co - co_base] : 0.0f; } } // Expand 3x3 + ReLU for (int co = 0; co < C_e2; co++) { float acc = b_e2[co]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { int sm_base = ci * SH * SW; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int w_offset = ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw; acc += sm_sq[sm_base + (sh + kh) * SW + sw + kw] * w_e2[w_offset]; } } } output[out_base + C_e1 + co] = acc > 0.0f ? acc : 0.0f; } } ``` Wait, I need to double-check the `#pragma unroll` on `for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++)`. If C_in=512, the compiler might try to unroll this loop 4 times (because I said `#pragma unroll 4`), which means the loop body is replicated 4 times and the loop iterates 128 times. This is reasonable and can improve instruction-level parallelism. For `for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++)`, since C_sq=16,32,48,64, I can use `#pragma unroll` without a number, and the compiler will fully unroll these small loops. This should be fine. For `for (int co = co_base; co < co_end; co++)` inside the C_sq loop, since co_end - co_base <= 32, the compiler might unroll it. But since the bounds are dynamic (co_end depends on co_base and C_e1), it might not fully unroll. But with `#pragma unroll`, it should try to unroll the inner loop. I added `#pragma unroll` there. For `for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++)` and `for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++)`, these are small constant loops, so `#pragma unroll` is good. One more thing: I replaced `sq_off` with `sq_spatial = sh * SW + sw` to avoid recomputing it in every loop. Good. Actually, in expand3x3: ```cpp for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { int sm_base = ci * SH * SW; for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { acc += sm_sq[sm_base + (sh + kh) * SW + sw + kw] * w_e2[w_offset]; } } } ``` Here, `(sh + kh) * SW + sw + kw` is computed for each `kw`. I can optimize this by precomputing `sh * SW + sw + kh * SW` before the `kw` loop: ```cpp for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { int sm_base = ci * SH * SW; for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { int sm_row = sm_base + (sh + kh) * SW + sw; for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int w_offset = ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw; acc += sm_sq[sm_row + kw] * w_e2[w_offset]; } } } ``` This might save a few instructions. Let me include this optimization. Similarly, for expand1x1, `sm_sq[ci * SH * SW + sq_spatial]` could be stored in a local variable if I process multiple `co` values. Actually, in the expand1x1 code: ```cpp for (int ci = 0; ci < C_sq; ci++) { float v = sm_sq[ci * SH * SW + sq_spatial]; for (int co = co_base; co < co_end; co++) { acc[co - co_base] += v * w_e1[co * C_sq + ci]; } } ``` Here, `v` is loaded once per `ci`. Good. Now let me also make sure the output channel loop for expand1x1 is correct. If C_e1=64 and CO_TILE=32: - co_base=0: co_end=32. acc[0..31] computed. - co_base=32: co_end=64. acc[0..31] computed for co=32..63. This is correct. One concern: does the compiler properly handle `acc[32]` as registers? With `#pragma unroll` on the inner `co` loop, and since `co_end - co_base <= 32` is bounded, the compiler should map `acc` to registers. For C_e1=192 or 256, we have 6 or 8 iterations of the co_base loop. Each iteration reuses the same `acc` registers. For expand1x1 with C_e1=256, C_sq=64: the inner `ci` loop runs 64 times. Each iteration does up to 32 FMAs on the `acc` array. This is 64*32 = 2048 FMAs per output pixel. A thread should be able to sustain this throughput. Wait, for C_e1=256 and C_sq=64, expand1x1 is 256*64 = 16,384 FMAs per pixel. If a block has 64 pixels and the GPU does 67 TFLOPS, that's 64*16,384 = ~1M FMAs per block. A warp can do 32 FMAs per cycle on H100 (I think). At 1.5GHz, a warp does 48 GFMA/s? Actually, H100 has much more throughput. Let me not worry too much about the exact throughput. assistantLet me analyze the model and GPU specs, then build the optimized kernel. **Architecture analysis:** - SqueezeNet-style with 8 Fire modules (squeeze 1x1 → expand 1x1 + expand 3x3) - Large input: 64×3×512×512 - Most compute is in 1×1 convs (GEMM-like) + 3×3 expands - Key inefficiency in reference: writes/reads intermediate squeeze outputs between layers, separate relu/cat kernels **Optimization strategy:** 1. **Fuse entire Fire modules** into single kernels: load input → squeeze+relu into shared memory → expand1x1+relu + expand3x3+relu → write concatenated output directly. This eliminates global memory traffic for intermediate squeeze activations, removes separate kernel launches, and fuses all conv-bias-relu operations. 2. **Custom classifier kernel** fusing conv1×1+relu. 3. Use PyTorch for the large initial 7×7 conv and maxpools where cuDNN is already optimal. **Kernel design details:** - Block size 8×8 = 64 threads - Shared memory: input channels (cooperative load) + squeeze outputs with halo for 3×3 - Channel-first layout `[C_sq][SH][SW]` for squeeze shared memory to eliminate bank conflicts during expand reads - Tile output channels in groups of 32 to control
call → compile_kernel

Yes, this is clean. For the 1x1 conv, same idea. Also, for the correctness of the kernel itself: I need to make sure the memory layout matches. The reference model uses NCHW. `nn.Conv2d` by default uses NCHW. In my kernel, I reconstructed using `input[((b * H + gh) * W + gw) * C_in + ci]`. For NCHW tensor `[N, C, H, W]`, strides are typically `[C*H*W, H*W, W, 1]`, so indexing `((b*H + h)*W + w)*C + ci` only works if the last dimension is C (i.e., NHWC), which is NOT PyTorch's default layout. PyTorch uses NCHW, so the data pointer for a tensor `[N, C, H, W]` has stride `[C*H*W, H*W, W, 1]`. The element at `[n, c, h, w]` is at offset `n*C*H*W + c*H*W + h*W + w`. If I use `.contiguous(memory_format=torch.channels_last)`, then the layout is NHWC, with stride `[C*H*W, 1, W*C, C]`. But by default, PyTorch tensors are contiguous in NCHW. So my indexing `((b*H + h)*W + w)*C_in + ci` assumes NHWC layout! That's wrong for PyTorch default. I need to fix the kernel to use NCHW indexing: - input offset for (b, ci, h, w): `b*C_in*H*W + ci*H*W + h*W + w` - Or equivalently: `(b*H + h)*W + w` is wrong because channel is the outer dimension. Correct NCHW indexing: `offset = b*C*H*W + c*H*W + h*W + w` For reading all channels at a spatial location (h, w): ```cpp for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { float v = input[b*C_in*H*W + ci*H*W + h*W + w]; } ``` This means when I load into shared memory, I should load per-channel for the spatial position. Actually, for shared memory, loading channel-first is fine but bank conflicts might occur. Let me restructure: For each spatial pixel in the tile (including halo), I load all C_in channels into sm_input sequentially. Since threads load different channels, and float arrays of length C_in are accessed with stride 1 by a single thread, that's fine. Layout: sm_input[ci] for channel ci at the current spatial location. But then when different threads access the same channel ci, they access the same shared memory bank (bank = ci % 32). Since each thread is computing a different output channel but might all need the same input channel, this causes bank conflicts. That's bad. Better: interleave channels in shared memory? Or use a different layout. Actually in the kernel: - The shared memory for input is accessed in the inner loop of the squeeze computation: `for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { acc += sm_input[ci] * weight[c * C_in + ci]; }` - All threads within a warp read `sm_input[0]`, `sm_input[1]`, etc. together but for different output channels. - `sm_input[ci]` is broadcast to all threads, which has no bank conflicts! Broadcasting from a shared memory location is conflict-free. Wait, that's true! Accessing the same shared memory address from all threads in a warp is broadcast and has no bank conflict. So reading `sm_input[ci]` by all threads simultaneously is actually optimal. What about writing: `for (int ci = tid; ci < C_in; ci += ntid) { sm_input[ci] = ...; }` — each thread writes a different address, no conflict. And for reading during expand: `float v = sm_sq[ci * SH * SW + sq_spatial];` — here, all threads in a warp access the same `ci` but different `sq_spatial`. Since `sq_spatial` differs by 1 (or small amount) and SH*SW might cause strided accesses... let's see: `sm_sq` layout: [C_sq][SH][SW] Access: `ci * SH * SW + sq_spatial` where `sq_spatial` is in range [0, SH*SW-1]. For threads in a warp, `sq_spatial` differs. If SH*SW = 100, and threads access points (h0,w0), (h0,w1), etc., the offsets differ by 1. Since each float is 4 bytes, offset 0, 1, 2, 3 maps to banks 0, 1, 2, 3. As long as the stride causes different banks (stride 1 = no conflict), this is fine. But for expand 3x3: `sm_sq[sm_row + kw]` where `kw` varies. For threads in a warp, each thread accesses its own spatial location in the center/around it. The `sm_row + kw` for thread 0 might be X, for thread 1 might be X+1, etc. With `stride=1`, these fall in consecutive banks, which is fine - no conflicts for a read pattern where each thread reads its own unique bank. So the memory layout is actually pretty good in shared memory. But the global memory indexing was wrong! Let me rewrite the kernels with correct NCHW indexing. For loading from input: `input[b * C_in * H * W + ci * H * W + gh * W + gw]` In kernel launch, I'll need B, H, W. Wait, PyTorch tensor sizes: - input: [B, C_in, H, W] - output: [B, C_e1+C_e2, H, W] (spatial size same because padding=1 on 3x3 and 1x1) For output: ```cpp output[b * (C_e1+C_e2) * H * W + co * H * W + h * W + w] ``` Actually, NCHW indexing. Let me rewrite the kernel carefully. Here's the plan for `cuda_sources`: ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template<int C_in, int C_sq, int C_e1, int C_e2> __global__ void fire_fused_kernel( const float* input, const float* w_sq, const float* b_sq, const float* w_e1, const float* b_e1, const float* w_e2, const float* b_e2, float* output, int B, int H, int W ) { // ... NCHW layout: // input[b, ci, h, w] = input[(b * C_in + ci) * H * W + h * W + w] // output[b, co, h, w] = output[(b * (C_e1+C_e2) + co) * H * W + h * W + w] } ``` Wait, `(b * C + c) * H * W + h * W + w` = `b*C*H*W + c*H*W + h*W + w`. Yes. Actually, `at::Tensor` data_ptr for NCHW contiguous is exactly this layout. Now for shared memory, since I load `sm_input[ci]` as "all channels at current spatial position", this works with NCHW because at position (h, w), channels are contiguous in memory: `base + ci * H * W`... wait, no. For spatial position (h, w), channel 0 is at offset `b*C*H*W + 0*H*W + h*W + w`. Channel 1 is at `b*C*H*W + 1*H*W + h*W + w`. The stride between channels is `H*W`, not 1. So channels are NOT contiguous at a single spatial location in NCHW. They are strided by H*W. Loading channel ci using: ```cpp input[base_spatial + ci * H * W] ``` where `base_spatial = b*C*H*W + h*W + w`. So for a fixed (b, h, w), we iterate over channels with stride `H*W`. For shared memory `sm_input[ci]`, we can store channels sequentially. The load code: ```cpp int spatial_offset = b * C_in * H * W + gh * W + gw; for (int ci = tid; ci < C_in; ci += ntid) { sm_input[ci] = input[spatial_offset + ci * H * W]; } ``` And for squeeze weight access: `w_sq[c * C_in + ci]` (weights are [C_out, C_in] contiguous). Then for expand 1x1 weights: `w_e1[co * C_sq + ci]`. And for expand 3x3 weights: `w_e2[((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw]`. Output writes: ```cpp int out_spatial_offset = b * (C_e1 + C_e2) * H * W + h * W + w; output[out_spatial_offset + co * H * W] = ... ``` Wait, for output at (b, h, w), channel co is at `b*C_out*H*W + co*H*W + h*W + w`. So writing `output[out_spatial_offset + co * H * W] = val` where `out_spatial_offset = b*C_out*H*W + h*W + w`. This is correct. OK now let me structure the code properly: `cuda_sources`: ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template<int C_in, int C_sq, int C_e1, int C_e2> __global__ void fire_fused_kernel(...) { // ... } template<int C_in, int C_out> __global__ void conv1x1_relu_kernel(...) { ... } #define DEFINE_FIRE_LAUNCHER(CIN, CSQ, CE1, CE2) \ void launch_fire_##CIN##_##CSQ##_##CE1##_##CE2( \ const float* input, const float* w_sq, const float* b_sq, \ const float* w_e1, const float* b_e1, const float* w_e2, const float* b_e2, \ float* output, int B, int H, int W) { \ dim3 block(8, 8); \ dim3 grid((W + 7) / 8, (H + 7) / 8, B); \ int sm_size = (CIN + 10 * 10 * CSQ) * sizeof(float); \ fire_fused_kernel<CIN, CSQ, CE1, CE2><<<grid, block, sm_size>>>( \ input, w_sq, b_sq, w_e1, b_e1, w_e2, b_e2, output, B, H, W); \ } ``` And `cpp_sources`: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> // Forward declarations of launchers void launch_fire_96_16_64_64(...); // ... void launch_conv1x1_relu_512_1000(const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int B, int H, int W); at::Tensor fire_forward_96_16_64_64(at::Tensor input, ...) { int B = input.size(0), H = input.size(2), W = input.size(3); auto out = torch::empty({B, 128, H, W}, input.options()); launch_fire_96_16_64_64(input.data_ptr<float>(), ..., out.data_ptr<float>(), B, H, W); return out; } ``` And `functions=['fire_forward_96_16_64_64', ...]`. This approach keeps all C++ `at::Tensor` handling in the g++-compiled `cpp_sources`, and only raw C / CUDA in the nvcc-compiled `cuda_sources`. This should eliminate ABI issues. Let me implement this now. I also need to make sure the kernel handles NCHW correctly. For shared memory in fire_fused_kernel: NCHW input load: ```cpp int spatial_offset = b * C_in * H * W + gh * W + gw; for (int ci = tid; ci < C_in; ci += ntid) { sm_input[ci] = input[spatial_offset + ci * H * W]; } ``` NCHW squeeze output write to shared memory: For squeeze, we are computing `sm_sq[c][sh][sw]`. We don't need to write to global memory. We just store in shared memory. The layout of `sm_sq` can be whatever is convenient. I'll use `sm_sq[c * SH * SW + sh * SW + sw]`. For expand 1x1, we read `sm_sq[ci * SH * SW + sq_spatial]`. For expand 3x3, we read similarly. Finally, output write: ```cpp int out_spatial_offset = b * (C_e1 + C_e2) * H * W + h * W + w; output[out_spatial_offset + co * H * W] = val; ``` Wait, I need to write to different output channels. For expand1x1, co ranges over [0, C_e1). For each co, write to channel co. For expand3x3, co ranges over [0, C_e2), write to channel C_e1 + co. So: ```cpp output[out_spatial_offset + co * H * W] = val > 0.0f ? val : 0.0f; ``` where `out_spatial_offset = b * (C_e1 + C_e2) * H * W + h * W + w`. This means all threads write to the same base spatial location but different channels. Since channels are strided by H*W, these are far apart in memory (assuming H*W > 1) and won't cause bank conflicts in L2. But for small H*W, there might be issues, but H*W is at least 14e4 for the first layer... no wait, after maxpool the sizes reduce. Let me check: Input: 512x512 → conv1 stride2: 253x253 → maxpool: 127x127 Fire1: 127x127 (H=127, W=127, H*W=16129) Fire2: same Fire3: same Maxpool: 64x64 (H=64, W=64, H*W=4096) Fire4: 64x64 Fire5,6,7: 64x64 Maxpool: 32x32 (H=32, W=32, H*W=1024) Fire8: 32x32 So for Fire8, H*W = 1024. Output channels = 512. So writing channels co*1024 apart. If co differs by 1, the offset differs by 4096 bytes (1024 floats). L2 cache line is 128 bytes. So consecutive channel writes are 4096 bytes apart, which means they map to completely different cache lines and banks. No contention at all. For global memory writes, the GPU coalesces accesses from a warp when they are to contiguous memory. Here, each thread in a warp writes to a completely different memory location (different channels for the same spatial pixel). This is NOT coalesced. A warp of 32 threads will write to 32 different cache lines, which is terrible for memory bandwidth. Oops! This is a critical issue. To have coalesced writes, we need the threads in a warp to write to contiguous memory. For NCHW, channels are strided by H*W, so for the same spatial pixel, contiguous channels are H*W apart. Two threads writing to channels 0 and 1 for the same pixel are writing to addresses 4096 bytes apart. This is bad. The fix: have threads in a warp write the SAME channel but at DIFFERENT spatial pixels (contiguous in memory). For example, thread (0,0) writes channel 0, pixel 0; thread (0,1) writes channel 0, pixel 1. But in our kernel, threads are mapped to spatial pixels (threadIdx.x -> w, threadIdx.y -> h). So in a warp, threads have varying w with the same h (or varies slowly). For a block of 8x8, a warp covers 8 w's and 4 h's (32 threads). Actually, with 8 threads in x and 8 in y, warp 0 might cover threads (0..7, 0..3) depending on the mapping. If threadIdx = y*8 + x, then a warp of 32 covers y=0, x=0..7 and y=1, x=0..7 and y=2, x=0..7 and y=3, x=0..7 — wait, that's 32 threads. No, 32 threads = 4 rows of 8 threads each. Warp 0: (0,0) to (0,7), (1,0) to (1,7), (2,0) to (2,7), (3,0) to (3,7)? No, 4*8=32. Yes. Warp 1: (4,0) to (7,7). For coalesced writes, we want threads in a warp to write to contiguous memory. If all threads write channel 0, then the memory addresses are `base + h*W + w`. For warp 0, w varies 0..7 and h varies 0..3. The addresses are `base + h*W + w`. For a given h and varying w, addresses are contiguous. But for different h, there's a gap of W-8. Since W is typically much larger than 8, this means the warp writes 4 separate contiguous segments of 8 floats each. This is partially coalesced (4 transactions per warp for channel 0). That's acceptable. Actually, modern GPUs can coalesce accesses from a warp even with gaps if the segments are aligned. For Ampere/Hopper, memory access granularity is 128 bytes. Each thread writes 4 bytes. A warp writes 128 bytes total. If the warp writes 4 groups of 8 contiguous floats aligned to 32 bytes, that's 4 segments of 32 bytes = 128 bytes total, potentially requiring 4 memory transactions. That's acceptable. But if we have threads in a warp each write a different channel, we get 32 separate cache lines. So it's much better to structure the kernel so threads write to the same channel. In my current kernel, each thread computes ALL output channels for its spatial pixel. This means each thread does a lot of compute but writes to many channels. The writes to different channels are strided by H*W, so non-coalesced. A better approach for NCHW on GPUs: compute one or a few output channels per thread, but have all threads in the block cooperate on spatial pixels. However, this requires loading all input spatial pixels for each channel group. This is the classic "channels-last is better for GPU compute on NCHW data unless you carefully tile by channel". Actually, in PyTorch, conv2d operations are heavily optimized with implicit GEMM, which converts NCHW to NHWC or uses specialized kernels. Custom elementwise/channelwise kernels often use the "one spatial pixel, all channels" approach because it fits the "reduce over channels" pattern (like LayerNorm). But for convolutions, writing output channels with strided access is bad. Alternative: use shared memory to stage output in NHWC layout, then transpose and write? That's complex and adds overhead. Another alternative: each block computes a tile of output channels for all spatial pixels in the tile. For example, block computes channels [co_start, co_end) for a 8x8 spatial tile. Then threads within a block are assigned to channels, and they write contiguous output for those channels. But with block size of 64 threads, and C_out up to 512, we'd need multiple blocks per spatial tile. Actually, we could use a 3D grid: (tile_w, tile_h, channel_group). But then the kernel needs to load all input channels for the spatial tile into shared memory, and different blocks would duplicate this load. That's also bad. For the squeeze layer, if each block handles channels [c_start, c_start+C_block) for the entire spatial tile, it's feasible. But for Fire module fusion, we need the squeeze output for all channels in shared memory anyway to feed expand. Wait, let me reconsider the memory access patterns. For expand1x1 output (C_e1 up to 256), the writes are: `output[b*C_out*H*W + co*H*W + h*W + w]` If a thread computes all C_e1 channels for one pixel, it's writing C_e1 different memory locations, each strided by H*W. If instead, multiple threads cooperate: thread t computes channel co = t for pixel (h,w), then thread 0 writes channel 0 for pixel (h,w), thread 1 writes channel 1 for pixel (h,w). These writes are NOT contiguous (strided by H*W), but they are predictable. The GPU's memory subsystem can handle strided writes with some efficiency, but it's not as good as coalesced. However, note: the total output size for the entire model is relatively small compared to the compute. The main cost is in the compute (MACs), not necessarily in writing the final output. Each Fire module writes its output once. Actually, let me compute the operational intensity. For a Fire module with C_in, C_sq, C_e1, C_e2: - Input read: B*C_in*H*W (once) - Squeeze weight read: C_sq * C_in (tiny) - Expand1x1 weight read: C_e1 * C_sq (tiny) - Expand3x3 weight read: C_e2 * C_sq * 3 * 3 (tiny) - Output write: B*(C_e1+C_e2)*H*W Total memory: ~B*(C_in + C_e1 + C_e2)*H*W * 4 bytes Total compute: B*H*W * (C_in*C_sq + C_sq*C_e1 + C_sq*C_e2*9) MACs Operational intensity = compute / memory ≈ C_sq * (C_in + C_e1 + 9*C_e2) / (C_in + C_e1 + C_e2) * 4 / (4 bytes per float) For typical values (C_in ~ 256, C_sq ~ 48, C_e1=C_e2=192): Intensity = 48 * (256 + 192 + 1728) / (256 + 384) = 48 * 2176 / 640 ≈ 163.2 FLOPs/byte. With H100 providing 3.35 TB/s bandwidth and 67 TFLOPS FP32, the compute-bounded threshold is 67e12 / 3.35e12 = 20 FLOPs/byte. Since our operational intensity is ~163, we are COMPUTE BOUNDED! Memory bandwidth is not the bottleneck here. The write pattern doesn't matter much because compute dominates. This means my kernel's non-coalesced writes won't significantly impact performance because the ALUs are busy. Fused kernels mainly win by eliminating memory round-trips for intermediate values (the squeeze activations), which is a memory-bound optimization for the intermediate steps. Actually, wait. The reference implementation also has a lot of compute, but its intermediate steps are memory-bound for the squeeze output because it writes/reads to global memory. The fused kernel avoids that completely. So my kernel should still be a big win even with non-coalesced writes for NCHW, because compute dominates. But to be safe, I can improve the write pattern. One way to improve writes: compute output channels in groups, and have each thread compute a subset of channels. But without changing the block structure too much... Actually, a simpler fix: have each thread compute one output channel for all spatial pixels? No, we have 64 threads and 512 output channels. Let me think of a better block configuration: - Block size: 8x8 = 64 threads. - For expand, each thread could compute 1 output channel for 1 spatial pixel. - But then we'd need 512 threads for 512 channels. Alternatively, keep the current kernel logic but note that the write bandwidth is not the bottleneck. Let me focus on getting the kernel to compile and work correctly first. For correctness, I need to make sure: 1. NCHW indexing is correct. 2. ReLU is applied correctly (inplace in reference but we compute fresh). 3. Maxpool and initial conv use PyTorch's implementations. Actually, for the first conv (7x7 stride 2) and maxpools, cuDNN is highly optimized, so using PyTorch is fine. For classifier, I fused conv1x1+ReLU. The original has `nn.Dropout(p=0.0)` (no-op), then `Conv2d` + `ReLU` + `AdaptiveAvgPool2d`. I replaced conv+relu with my kernel, then adaptiveavgpool2d. That's correct. Let me write the full code now, carefully. Actually, before I write all that, let me reconsider if `__cxa_call_terminate` was actually because I passed `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources` with type mismatches or missing `extern "C"`. Let me try the proper split between cpp and cuda. But actually, the simpler reason could be that the build succeeds but `.so` is invalid. Let me think if I can just avoid `cpp_sources` completely and still have host functions in the `.cu` file but with extra flags to fix the ABI. Actually, looking back at the compile output, nvcc used: ``` -ccbin /opt/conda/bin/x86_64-conda-linux-gnu-cc ``` and g++ used `/usr/bin/g++`. They might be different GCC versions! The `x86_64-conda-linux-gnu-cc` (from conda) might be GCC 11 or 12, and `/usr/bin/g++` might be GCC 9. If the C++ ABI differs, linking them together causes issues like undefined `__cxa_call_terminate`. This is likely the root cause! The two compilers have different standard library versions. Solution: make sure both files are compiled with the same compiler, or avoid any C++ standard library usage in the file compiled by the other compiler. If I define the host functions in `cpp_sources` compiled by `/usr/bin/g++`, and `cuda_sources` compiled by nvcc with `x86_64-conda-linux-gnu-cc`, any object crossing between them with incompatible C++ stdlib will fail. But if the `.cu` file contains ONLY C-style functions (no `at::Tensor`, no standard C++), and the `.cpp` file contains all the C++/pybind11 code, there should be no ABI issue because the boundary is plain C (or CUDA C but only raw pointers). This confirms that I MUST put the host `at::Tensor` functions in `cpp_sources` and keep `cuda_sources` as pure C/CUDA with no torch C++ API. Let me proceed with this architecture. Now, for the `cpp_sources`, I need `torch::empty` to allocate output tensors. That's fine because it's compiled with PyTorch's toolchain. For `cuda_sources`, I'll define C-style launch functions that take raw pointers. Now, writing the full code:

PyTorch uses [C_sq, C_in, 1, 1], and when I do `.contiguous()`, it should flatten to [C_sq, C_in, 1, 1] = [C_sq, C_in] effectively. Wait no - contiguous preserves the shape, but the data is stored in row-major order. So `w_sq.data_ptr<float>()[c * C_in + ci]` is correct for accessing weight[c, ci, 0, 0]. For expand3x3: `w_e2[w_offset]` where `w_offset = ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw`. PyTorch weight is [C_e2, C_sq, 3, 3]. The index would be `((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw`. I said: ```cpp int spatial_offset = b * C_in * H * W + gh * W + gw; ... input[spatial_offset + ci * H * W] ``` In NCHW, the correct index for [b, ci, gh, gw] is: `((b * C_in + ci) * H + gh) * W + gw = b * C_in * H * W + ci * H * W + gh * W + gw` So `spatial_offset + ci * H * W` where `spatial_offset = b * C_in * H * W + gh * W + gw` gives: `b * C_in * H * W + gh * W + gw + ci * H * W` But the correct formula is: `b * C_in * H * W + ci * H * W + gh * W + gw` These ARE THE SAME! `gh * W + gw + ci * H * W` = `ci * H * W + gh * W + gw`. Yes! So my indexing IS correct for NCHW. Phew. So the layout is fine. What else could cause the difference? Actually wait - there's a potential issue with `torch::empty` vs zero-initialization. PyTorch's GEMM-based conv2d uses optimized BLAS which may accumulate in different order, and might use FMA instructions. The result can differ slightly. My kernel handles the 3x3 padding by loading a padded tile. Let me verify the expand3x3 computation. In the reference: ```python x = self.squeeze_activation(self.squeeze(x)) return torch.cat([ self.expand1x1_activation(self.expand1x1(x)), self.expand3x3_activation(self.expand3x3(x)) ], 1) ``` For expand3x3, padding=1, so output spatial dims are same as input spatial dims. My kernel computes expand3x3 for each output pixel using the neighborhood in shared memory. The shared memory has size (BH+2) × (BW+2), which is exactly the padded tile needed for 3x3 convolution with stride 1 and padding 1. For pixel at (h, w) in the output, corresponding to (sh, sw) = (threadIdx.y+1, threadIdx.x+1) in shared memory, the 3x3 neighborhood is: - sh-1 to sh+1 - sw-1 to sw+1 In my code: ```cpp for (int kh = -1; kh <= 1; ++kh) { for (int kw = -1; kw <= 1; ++kw) { int w_offset = ((co * C_sq + ci) * 3 + (kh + 1)) * 3 + (kw + 1); acc += sm_sq[sm_base + kh * SW + kw] * w_e2[w_offset]; } } ``` Here `sm_base = (ci * SH + sh) * SW + sw`, so `sm_base + kh * SW + kw` accesses `(ci, sh + kh, sw + kw)`. That's correct for the neighborhood. Wait, but there's something subtle. For the 3x3 weight indexing: PyTorch uses `[out_channels, in_channels, 3, 3]` and the weight at position `(co, ci, kh, kw)` corresponds to the kernel offset `(kh, kw)` where `kh` goes from 0 to 2. In my code, `kh` ranges from -1 to 1, and I map it to weight index `kh + 1` (0 to 2). But does the spatial position match? In standard conv2d with padding=1, the output at (h, w) is computed from input at (h-1+h_k, w-1+w_k) for h_k, w_k in [0, 2]. In my shared memory, `sh = threadIdx.y + 1` corresponds to `h` in global coordinates (since tile_h = blockIdx.y * BH, and h = tile_h + threadIdx.y). The shared memory was loaded with `gh = tile_h - 1 + sh_mem_index`, so: - For `sh_mem = 0`, `gh = tile_h - 1` (one above tile) - For `sh_mem = 1`, `gh = tile_h` - etc. - For `sh_mem = sh = threadIdx.y + 1`, `gh = tile_h - 1 + threadIdx.y + 1 = tile_h + threadIdx.y = h` So `sh_mem = sh` in shared memory corresponds to global `h`. Then `sh_mem + kh` for `kh = -1` corresponds to `h - 1`, which is what we want for the 3x3 kernel. And the weight position: the kernel element that multiplies input at `(h-1, w)` should be `weight[co, ci, 0, ...]`. In my code, for `kh = -1`, `w_offset` uses `(kh + 1) = 0`. That's correct. So the math seems right. But wait - PyTorch's `nn.Conv2d` by default uses `groups=1` and `bias=True`. Let me check: yes, the reference model has bias (default is True in PyTorch). Hmm, let me think about whether the difference could be due to how I'm handling the `.contiguous()` call on the weights. When I call `.contiguous()` on a tensor, if it's already contiguous (which Conv2d weights are by default), it returns the same tensor. The data layout is `[out_channels, in_channels, *kernel_size]`. So for 1x1 conv, it's `[C_out, C_in, 1, 1]`. When I index with `w_sq[c * C_in + ci]`, I'm flattening the last two dimensions (which are both 1) and getting the correct element. Wait, what about the NCHW output layout? My kernel writes to output as: `output[out_spatial_offset + co * H * W]` where `out_spatial_offset = b * C_out * H * W + h * W + w` In NCHW, the correct index for [b, co, h, w] is: `((b * C_out + co) * H + h) * W + w = b * C_out * H * W + co * H * W + h * W + w` So `out_spatial_offset + co * H * W` should be `b * C_out * H * W + h * W + w + co * H * W = b * C_out * H * W + co * H * W + h * W + w`. That's correct! ✓ OK the indexing is all correct. Another thought: channel ordering in the output. The reference does `torch.cat([expand1x1(x), expand3x3(x)], dim=1)`. In PyTorch, a Conv2d with kernel_size=3, the weight at[:, :, 0, 0] corresponds to the top-left element of the kernel. When applied to input with padding=1, the top-left weight element multiplies the "northwest" neighbor of the center pixel. But in standard correlation/convolution terminology, it's actually cross-correlation (not convolution with flipped kernel). PyTorch's nn.Conv2d does cross-correlation. For cross-correlation at output position (h, w): `output[h, w] = sum(weight[kh, kw] * input[h + kh - padding, w + kw - padding])` With padding=1: `output[h, w] = sum(weight[kh, kw] * input[h + kh - 1, w + kw - 1])` for kh, kw in [0, 2] So `input[h - 1, w - 1]` (northwest) is multiplied by `weight[0, 0]`. `input[h + 1, w + 1]` (southeast) is multiplied by `weight[2, 2]`. But in PyTorch, `h - 1` should map to `weight[0]` only if `kh = 0` means "one row above center". Yes! In PyTorch's cross-correlation, `kh = 0` means the kernel element that multiplies `input[h - 1, w]`. That IS the north neighbor.
